==PAGE:5==
Abstract
For centuries, arches have been used to optimise load carrying capacity but are limited by
their extensive scaffolding and challenging construction works. Technological advanceВӯ
ments have lead to the development of PearlВӯChain (PC) bridges, a closed spandrel conВӯ
crete arch bridge. The design consists of straight preВӯfabricated SuperВӯLight concrete
decks (SLВӯdeck) postВӯtensioned together to form an arch, fill material above the arch and
a postВӯtensioned concrete top plate. Taking the PC technology further, studies have been
investigating the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept, a structural concept utilising the strength
and stiffness of the fill material and concrete top plate when calculating the loadВӯcarrying
capacity of the PCВӯBridge. Although increased capacities were found, challenges were
identified.
This thesis builds on findings from previous studies and computes analytical calculations
to investigate identified challenges of the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept. The optimal
configuration of the elements used in the Sandwich Arch Bridge is proposed based on
capacities, environmental impact and construction process. Calculations are done to find
and visually present the capacity and response of the Sandwich Arch Bridge. Relevant
loads relating to the erection procedure and the final usage of the bridge is identified and
combined into critical load combinations. Investigation of causes and analytical represenВӯ
tation of splitting between Sandwich Arch Bridge layers is conducted. The competitiveВӯ
ness of the Sandwich Arch Bridge design is evaluated against a commonly used concrete
bridge structure regarding sustainability and the construction process.
The thesis concludes that the identified challenges are not a limitation for the construction
of Sandwich Arch Bridges. Implementing the PCВӯArch as a circular two hinged arch with
a riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio between 1/10Вӯ1/14 is seen as favourable. Optimal SLВӯdeck properties
are found to reduce the previously seen compression cracking from postВӯtensioning. VisuВӯ
alisation of PCВӯarch total weight with mobile crane lifting capacities is computed to be used
in the preliminary design for reduced construction cost. Multiple explanations regarding
previously seen splitting force between layers were investigated. All splitting forces are
based on finding vertical components between the layers based on bending moments
from external live loads. The capacities of the Sandwich Arch Bridge are based on the
capacity of each element. Therefore, the internal forces are found in each element and
evaluated based on individual capacities. Critical load cases for the PCВӯArch was from
the skew filling procedure and total bridge dead load. Critical load cases for the Sandwich
Arch Bridge was LM1 applied at the quarterВӯpoint and midВӯspan. The environmental imВӯ
pact of the Sandwich Arch Bridge was lower, with relative savings increasing significantly
with increasing spans above 27m. Construction time was found to be approximately 45%
less for the Sandwich Arch Bridge.
This thesis adds to previous studies and academic literature on arch bridges using SuperВӯ
Light structures with PearlВӯChain Technology. This thesis complements the literature by
focusing on analytical investigations of previously identified challenges and analytical repВӯ
resentation of the response and capacity of the complex Sandwich Arch Bridge structure.
Keywords: SuperВӯLight Structure вҖ“ PearlВӯChain Technology вҖ“ SLВӯdeck вҖ“ PearlВӯChain
Bridge вҖ“ Sandwich Arch Bridge вҖ“ Analytical Investigation

1
Introduction
1.1
Background
When designing a bridge today, the objectives are not only based on the structuresвҖҷ abilВӯ
ity to resist the relevant loads during its intended lifetime but also to optimise the deВӯ
sign regarding environmental impact and construction time. At the Technical University of
Denmark (DTU), technological efforts have been pursued to reduce material energy conВӯ
sumption and CO2 emission combined with evaluated construction emission. This work
has resulted in a new patented structural concept by Professor Kristian Dahl Hertz, called
SuperВӯLight (SL) Structures, whose purpose is to create structures with high stiffness and
low weight [2]. Implementation of the SL concept in concrete structures has the potential
of reducing the worlds total CO2 emission from cement production from 5% to only 2%
[3].
The SL concept was used in the innovation of the SuperВӯLight deck (SLВӯdeck). The SLВӯ
deck is a concrete deck that combines regular concrete with lightВӯaggregate concrete
resulting in a light but still strong concrete deck. The SLВӯDeck is used in constructions
as floor slabs, and tests have been done demonstrating high loadВӯbearing capacity, high
sound insulating properties, and high fire resistance [4]. Further studies were done by AsВӯ
sistant Professor Philip Skov Halding, investigating the possibility of optimising the shape
and geometry of a bridge to applied load by using postВӯtensioned SLВӯDecks as a concrete
arch (PCВӯArch) [5], utilising the PearlВӯChain concept [6].
The firstВӯever PearlВӯChain (PC) Bridge was built in Denmark in 2015 as a filled closedВӯ
spandrel arch bridge with PCВӯArch, pervious concrete filling and concrete top plate. The
arches were positioned on site in half a working day, reintroducing the archВӯshape as
economically competitive and saving a considerable amount of CO2 regarding material
use and construction time. The capacity calculations of the PCВӯBridge only included the
arch alone. Suppose the strength and stiffness of the pervious concrete filling and the
concrete road slab could be utilised in the archвҖҷs loadВӯcarrying capacity. In that case, it
could result in even slimmer construction, less material use, and reduced CO2 emissions.
If the arch bridgeвҖҷs top plate is preВӯstressed when cast and the fill material could be found
to transfer shear forces, the arch bridge could be seen as a sandwich structure, taking
pressure in the top plate and tension in the arch. The resulting capacity of the arch bridge
could be considerably increased if the sandwich concept is present.
Concrete top deck
PC-arch
Fill material
Figure 1.1: Sandwich Arch Bridge design
Sandwich Arches
1
==PAGE:20==
1.2
Scope
This thesis aims to investigate previously found challenges regarding Sandwich Arch
Bridges. Both PCВӯArches and sandwich structures have been investigated, designed
and tested in full scale before. Increased capacities have been found in tests for SandВӯ
wich Arch Bridge structure with PCВӯArches, but challenges were seen in the structures
response and complex analytical representation.
The hypotheses of the overall thesis related to the studies were:
вҖў It is possible to construct Sandwich Arch Bridges when having identified and
addressed a number of structural challenges
Based on the hypotheses, the following objectives were identified:
вҖў Propose an optimal PearlВӯChain arch configuration to be implemented in Sandwich
Arch Bridge structures based on previous studies and analytical investigation.
вҖў Determine critical activities regarding the PearlВӯchain arch capacity relating to the
erection procedure.
вҖў Determine the critical loads and load combinations relating to the sandwich arch
structures capacity.
вҖў Investigate how a Sandwich Arch BridgeвҖҷs response and capacity can be repreВӯ
sented analytically
вҖў Investigate causes for splitting between layers as seen in previous studies and how
they can be represented analytically.
вҖў Investigate the Sandwich Arch Bridge competitiveness against a commonly used
bridge structures regarding sustainability and construction process.
In the thesis, the following subjects are considered to be outside the scope:
вҖў Deflection control
вҖў Climate loads on the structure like temperature, wind and snow.
вҖў Dynamic calculations.
1.3
Thesis Guide
The thesis contains a range of analytical calculations and discussions regarding chalВӯ
lenges related to the objectives of the work. The following points are given to present the
reader with an overview of the subjects within each chapter.
вҖў Chapter 2 presents relevant theory regarding subjects used and discussed further in
the thesis. Arch bridges are introduced with basic theory and statics. PC technology
and SLВӯdeck theory are given, all relevant for use in a sandwich structure. Sandwich
Arch Bridge theory, along with challenges, are introduced.
вҖў Chapter 3 includes analytical calculations on subjects related to the configuration of
a PCВӯArch to be used in a Sandwich Arch Bridge structure. Results from calculations
are discussed, and configurations are chosen for an optimal PCВӯArch.
вҖў Chapter 4 presents the Sandwich Arch Bridge erection procedure and critical stages
related to the erection process. Different Sandwich Arch Bridge design configuraВӯ
tions are presented, which are to be used in the following chapters.
2
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:21==
вҖў Chapter 5 Presents relevant loads and load combinations related to the design
calculations of a Sandwich Arch Bridge. The structures response to relevant loads
is calculated and presented.
вҖў Chapter 6 presents analytical capacity calculations for relevant Sandwich Arch Bridge
design configurations affected by relevant loads and load combinations.
вҖў Chapter 7 presents a bridge design comparison of the resulting Sandwich Arch
Bridge design configuration and a commonly used concrete bridge design. ComВӯ
parisons are made based on analytical calculations related to sustainability and the
construction process.

2.1
Arch Bridges
An arch bridge is defined as a vertically curved and axially compressed structural member
spanning an opening and providing support for moving loads above the opening [7]. Arch
structures can withstand vertical loading as compression in the arch, which is beneficial
when using a material with high compression capacities. Arches are solid and robust
structures that can last for centuries. Arch bridges can be classified in several ways
relating to the relative position of the arch and deck, as illustrated in Figure 2.1. The
most common arch bridge configurations are Deck Arch Bridge, Through Arch Bridge
and Half Through Arch Bridge. The Sandwich Arch Bridge can be classified as a Deck
Arch Bridge.
(a) Deck arch bridge
(b) Through arch bridge
(c) Half through arch bridge
Figure 2.1: Arch bridge classification
The space between the deck and arch in the deck arch bridge configuration is called
the spandrel. For a Sandwich Arch Bridge, the spandrel is closed and filled with a load
transferring material. Figure 2.2 illustrates a filled spandrel arch bridge where the rise f
is the height of the arch, L is the span of the arch, the crown is the top point of the arch,
the centre line is the shape line of the arch and the springings are the foundation of the
arch connected to the abutments.
Sandwich Arches
5
==PAGE:24==
Span L
Rise f
Arch center line
Springing
Crown
Figure 2.2: Spandrel arch design
The loads occurring on the bridge deck are transferred through the fill material onto the
arch. Loads on an arch bridge are carried outward along the archвҖҷs curve to the supports
at each end resulting in both horizontalВӯ and vertical reaction force at the supports. The
load transfer is illustrated in Figure 2.3 below.
RV
RH
RV
RH
Figure 2.3: Spandrel arch load transfer
2.1.1
History
Since ancient times, arches have been used in structures because of the ability to transfer
loads relating to their shape. The structural use of arches can be traced back to 4000 BC
to the Sumerians who lived in the TigrisВӯEuphrates Valley. By the time of the Romans,
most bridges were constructed as masonry arches [7]. The Romans developed empirical
rules for dimensioning the shape of the arch and the wedgeВӯshaped stone. One of the
most famous still standing arch structure tracing back to Roman times is the Pont du Gard
in France (Figure 2.4 [8]). The firstВӯever spandrel arch bridge in the world is the Zhaozhou
Bridge (Anji Bridge) in China, completed in 605 AD [7] (Figure 2.5 [9]). In the eighteenth
century, iron was for the first time used in bridges, and throughout the nineteenth century,
advanced steel/iron arch bridges were built. The emergence of concrete bridges was at
the end of the nineteenth century. The weekВӯinВӯtension but strongВӯinВӯcompression nature
of concrete makes it perfectly suitable for arch bridges, which is mostly in compression
[7].
6
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:25==
Figure 2.4: Le Pont du Gard [8]
Figure 2.5: Zhaozhou Bridge [9]
In many stone bridges and early concrete bridges, the carriageway was placed on fill
material of lesser quality than the material used in the arch itself but capable of transferring
loads. [10]. In the twentieth century, more and more concrete arch bridges were built. In
1904 the Risorgimento Bridge in Rome (Figure 2.6 [11]) was built as the first concrete arch
bridge with a span over 100m [7]. In presentВӯday arch structures, the bridge deck would
either be supported by columns transferring the vertical loads to the arch or be suspended
from hangers fixed to the arch [10]. The largest deck concrete arch bridge to date is the
Qinglong Railway Bridge in China (Figure 2.7 [12]) with a span of 445m with the bridge
deck supported by columns on the arch.
Figure 2.6: Risorgimento Bridge [11]
Figure 2.7: Qinglong Railway Bridge [12]
Concrete arches were a primary bridge type for a long time. However, as bridge techВӯ
nologies have advanced, more and more preВӯstressed concrete girders, steelВӯconcrete
composite girder, and steel girder bridges have been constructed [7]. The construction of
concrete arch bridges has been influenced by extensive scaffolding and challenging conВӯ
struction works, making their construction more time consuming and less economically
beneficial. Concurrently, with the development of preВӯstressed concrete, arch bridges
have become rarer, and today they are mainly applied for spans of more than 100m [10].
Modern era use of steelВӯconcrete composite and the utilisation of highВӯperformance conВӯ
crete are significant advantages in the arch bridge design, and construction [7], which in
combination with precast elements and standardised scaffolding elements may prove to
be competitive and advantageous for smaller span bridges [10].
Looking at a closedВӯspandrel arch bridge as a sandwich structure, transferring the shear
force between the top plate and the arch does not seem to have been done before. If
Sandwich Arches
7
==PAGE:26==
the sandwich concept is evident, the closedВӯspandrel arch structure could be optimised,
reducing material consumption further reducing construction time and project cost.
2.2
Arch Statics
The static characteristic of a perfectly formed arch structure is its ability to transform vertiВӯ
cal gravity load to axial compression. The arch structureвҖҷs ability to transfer loads depends
on multiple variables but is in this thesis categorised within the hinge configuration and
arch shape.
2.2.1
Arch Shape
The shape of the arch is determined by the rise, span and centre line shape. The riseВӯtoВӯ
span ratio is the ratio between the rise, f, and the span, L, as illustrated in Figure 2.2. The
ratio is an essential parameter in the arch configuration, affecting the structural behaviour
of the arch. Generally, neither a very steep nor a very flat arch is particularly desirable
from a combined assessment that incorporates economy, appearance and difficulties in
the execution [10]. An arch with a small ratio will develop relatively large compression
forces in the arch resulting in increased horizontal thrust at the abutment. An arch with a
large ratio develops smaller horizontal thrust but increased bending moments. An analysis
of 58 concrete arch bridges with lengths over 180m built between 1942 and 2011 found
that 95% had a spanВӯtoВӯrise ratio in the range of 2.3 to 8.0 [13] similar to a riseВӯtoВӯspan
ratio of
1
2.3 to 1
8. Salonga and Gauvreau [14] proposed a threshold ratio value of
1
20,
beyond which there is a rapid decrease in arch behaviour, causing the arch system to
carry significant portions of permanent loads in bending, rather than in compression. For
concrete, which has high compressive capacities, arch bridges with a small spanВӯtoВӯrise
could be beneficial for the overall capacity [7].
Different arch central line shapes have been used in arch bridge design, such as parabolic,
circular, elliptical or catenary. A perfect arch is defined as an arch in which only compresВӯ
sive force is acting at the central line of the arch. However, it is practically impossible to
have a perfect arch in bridge structures due to the multiple loadings or actions, which will
produce bending moment in the arch rib in addition to axial compression. An example of a
perfect arch central line shape is a parabolic arch subjected only by a uniform distributed
load. A circular arch shape is easily configured, due to the same shape along the length
of the arch, but the axis differs from the permanent thrust line causing nonВӯlinear stresses
along the arch rib.
The shape functions calculate the height of the arch at a distance, x, from the support. The
functions are derived by the arch characteristicsНҫ span L and rise f, The shape function
for a circular arch yc(x) is given in Equation 2.1 with rc being the radius in the circle,
given in Equation 2.2. The parabolic shape function yp(x) is given in Equation 2.3 and the
elliptical ye(x) in Equation 2.4. The catenary shape function, yca(x), is the graph of the
hyperbolic cosine function based on an inverted cable resting between two points in the
same height with equal distributed mass along the cable as given in Equation 2.5. The
factor Cca is found by taking the root of the shape function at the support with the help of
the given arch characteristics, as given in Equation 2.6.

2.2.2
Hinges
The arch bridge can be classified as threeВӯhinged, twoВӯhinged or fixed based on the numВӯ
ber of hinges used in the structure. Figure 2.9 illustrates the location of hinges in the
different classifications. A threeВӯhinged arch is a statically determinate structure, which
makes it easier to calculate. A twoВӯhinged arch is indeterminate to one degree of freedom.
A fixed arch is indeterminate to three degrees of freedom.
(a) ThreeВӯhinged arch
(b) TwoВӯhinged arch
(c) Fixed arch
Figure 2.9: Arch hinge configuration
Sandwich Arches
9
==PAGE:28==
The archвҖҷs sensitivity to secondary effects, such as arch shortening, foundation settleВӯ
ment, yielding of supports, and temperature variations, increases with its degree of static
indeterminacy. Therefore, as hinges are added to arches, the secondary effect decrease
[7]. For a threeВӯhinged arch, the arch shortening will cause a reduction in the rise and thus
an increase in the horizontal thrust. For twoВӯhinged and fixed arches, arch shortening or
elongation will usually reduce or increase the horizontal thrust [10].
Load Response
The arch structure will respond differently to load types depending on both the arch shape
and hinge configuration. Critical loads on arch structures are rarely caused by uniform
distributed loads but rather by a nonВӯsymmetrical load. A uniformly distributed load causes
primarily increased axial compression and limited bending moment. A nonВӯsymmetrical
load could be represented by a point load at 1
4L or a uniform distributed load on half of the
span, causing positive bending moments under the load position and negative moment
on the counterpart of the arch.
To illustrate the different responses, calculations of bending moment and normal force
thrust in the arch is done for a threeВӯ and twoВӯhinged circular, parabolic, elliptic and cateВӯ
nary arch with length L=30m and rise f=3.
The bending moment is calculated as a function where the forces acting on the arch
is calculated in the distance x from the support. For a two hinged arch, the unitВӯload
method is used, described in subsection 5.3.1. Figure 2.10 illustrates an arch loaded with
uniform load q and non symmetrical loads qh and point load P. The resulting moment at
a distance x from the support is the combination of the load and the reaction forces RV.A
and RH.A and are different for each shape function y(x). See Equation 2.7 to 2.9 for the
resulting moment equations. The reaction forces used in calculating the bending moment
are different depending on the chosen arch centre line shape and hinge configuration.

The arch structure requires good support and foundation properties. The static system
of the arch depends on the foundation to take large horizontal forces. Small foundation
settings could also lead to even more significant horizontal contributions from the arch.
However, the static systems used in this thesis assumes pinned supports.
2.3
PearlВӯChain Technology
Traditional arch bridges and other curved concrete structures require advanced scaffoldВӯ
ing techniques using curved moulds and extensive amounts of labour when constructed.
Since the price of labour has increased significantly over the years, it is no longer ecoВӯ
nomically beneficial to construct these curved structures. Therefore, it has been essential
to look for other techniques of erecting curved structures, hence the idea of PearlВӯChain
technologies.
PC technology was invented by Professor Kristian Dahl Hertz at DTU in 2008 [15]. The
technology is based on postВӯtensioning straight prefabricated concrete elements together
with a steel cable, which is anchored at both ends of the element chain. The tensioning
cable is installed perpendicular to each element end, leading to the joints between them
being subject to pure compression and the structure resisting tension as unloaded comВӯ
pression. If the concrete elements are cast with a slight angle at both ends where they
meet the next element, they can form various curved structures when tensioned together.
The cable ducts within the concrete elements are installed with curvature so that the steel
wire distributes the postВӯtensioning evenly without applying any sharp edges to the steel
wire [15]. An example of a PC structure is presented in Figure 2.15.
Sandwich Arches
13
==PAGE:32==
Prefab concrete
elements with cable duct
Post-tensioning cable
Figure 2.15: PearlВӯChain example
The concrete elements used in the PC structure can be straight and can therefore be proВӯ
duced with standard scaffolding and made to fit any arch geometry only by changing the
endsвҖҷ inclination. This opens up the possibility for standardised production. The overall
structure is also divided into smaller elements that are easy to store and transport to the
construction site [15]. Even though PC technology can be used for various geometries,
the main shape focused on in this report is regarding the PCВӯArch.
2.3.1
PearlВӯChain Arches
The PC technology enables the production of arches used as the bearing structure for
bridges without expensive scaffolding. The arch elements are prefabricated in perfect
conditions as straight elements and only form an arch once they are tensioned together.
Suppose the arch is designed as a circular shape with constant curvature. In that case,
the concrete elements can be produced with identical lengths and inclinations at the ends,
making all concrete elements identical. This further enables even more standardised
production.
The traditional inВӯsitu cast concrete bridge requires 28 days of curing the concrete. During
this period, the scaffolding must be present, taking up space and limiting access underВӯ
neath the bridge. This can be a big problem concerning bridges crossing traffic roads
where the traffic needs to be directed around the bridge or stopped for long periods. This
also leads to more CO2Вӯemissions from the vehicles queuing or having to take a detour.
On the other hand, the PCВӯArch can be assembled close to the working site and lifted in
place after assembly. The lifting process can be done in around 4Вӯ5 hours and at night
when there is less traffic [5]. After the PCВӯArch is lifted into place, the road underneath
can be opened again as usual. Once the PCВӯArch is erected, the contractor can continue
assembling the structure above the arch without affecting the traffic underneath.
2.4
SLВӯdeck
The SLВӯdeck is a preВӯstressed concrete slab combining Light Aggregate Concrete (LAC),
with a compressive strength of 3MPa and density of 700kg/m3, with a selfВӯcompacting
concrete with a compressive strength of 55 MPa and a density of 2400kg/m3. The LAC
is cast with a unique curved geometry leaving both lengthwise and transversal grooves.
The selfВӯcompacting concrete constitutes a ribbed slab surrounded by an insulated and
stabilising layer of lightweight concrete. PreВӯstressed reinforcement is placed in the slabвҖҷs
14
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:33==
lengthwise grooves, and regular reinforcement is placed in transversal grooves, establishВӯ
ing a crossВӯreinforced structure. The SLВӯdeck can be produced in different length, and
widths with standard heights of 180mm, 220mm, 270mm and 320mm [16]. The standard
SLВӯdeck is illustrated in Figure 2.16 [16].
Figure 2.16: SLВӯdeck [16].
The current standard production width of SLВӯdecks in Denmark is 2.4m with a total of six
LAC blocks of width 375mm as shown in Figure 2.17a. The height of the LAC blocks can
vary, but the minimum concrete thickness above is 40mm. The number of preВӯstressed
reinforcement strands can vary with top layer minimum 30mm coverage from top to the
centerline of the strand, midВӯlayer minimum 60mm and bottom layer minimum 92mm from
bottom to the centerline. The LAC blocks are 600mm in the longitudinal direction. The SLВӯ
Deck need a minimum of 200mm at the ends for preВӯstressing anchoring. The SLВӯdeck
section is illustrated in Figure 2.17.

acteristic ultimate strength of 1860 MPa. The preВӯstressing strands are stressed at the
production facility with high precision. The resulting stress in the tendon at delivery is
assumed to be 70% of the 0.1% proof strength of steel after all the relevant losses.

To transfer shear force between adjacent PCВӯArches, hammerhead joints are used along
the length of the arch. The jointвҖҷs design was calculated for use in a fullВӯscale load test of
PCВӯArches and was found to work as intended, transferring forces between arches [18].
The connection is formed by creating recesses in the side of the SLВӯdeck, 100mm deep
16
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:35==
and wide. For SLВӯdecks connected using hammerhead joints, the minimum width on the
sides of the LAC blocks is increased by 50mm, resulting in total minimum width of 100mm.
For conservative capacity calculations, the effective width of the SLВӯdeck, bSL.e, is used
where the additional width for the hammerhead joint is neglected. For load calculations,
the actual width of the SLВӯdeck, bSL.a, is used.
The configuration of the SLВӯdeck as a composite beam with three materials, all with varyВӯ
ing elasticity modulus, results in different section properties throughout the length of the
deck. In a PCВӯArch, additional section parameters are found in the joint section between
decks. Relevant section properties are found using YoungвҖҷs modulus (EВӯmodulus) ratio
for materials in the section investigated. Figure 2.19 annotates the areas and distances
of elements in the SLВӯdeck to be used in section parameters calculations.

2.5
Sandwich Arch Bridge
The Sandwich Arch Bridge consists of a PCВӯArch in the bottom, a filling layer above the
arch, and a postВӯtensioned top plate on top [15]. The idea is that the PCВӯArch should
withstand the dead loads of the bridge, but whenever the bridge is subject to live loads,
the filling material should be able to transfer shear loads so that the arch and top plate
can work as pair loads with the whole filling height as a lever arm.
Post-tensioned concrete bridge deck
PC-arch
Pervious concrete fill
Figure 2.20: Sandwich arch example
The whole idea behind the Sandwich Arch Bridge came as a product of the work done
by Philip Skov Halding when he designed and constructed a fully working PCВӯBridge in
2015 as a part of his PhD work at DTU [5]. This bridge used one full PCВӯArch and two half
arches as the bearing structure. The void above the arches was filled with a select type
of aggregate concrete that could drain any moisture in the bridge and guide it down to the
arches where the water could escape through preВӯmade openings. Due to soft soil at the
bridgeвҖҷs location, the foundations could not withstand any greater horizontal loads. This
problem was fixed by casting a bridge deck on top of the filling and postВӯtensioning the
deck to take up the half archesвҖҷ horizontal loads. After the bridge was finished, Halding
Sandwich Arches
17
==PAGE:36==
wanted to investigate if the filling material was strong enough to transfer shear between
the top plate and arch.
This question initiated the master thesis Development and test of arch bridge with maВӯ
terials of different stiffness [19] at DTU in 2015. The goal was to build a Sandwich Arch
Bridge prototype, apply loads to it, and monitor the effects of the sandwich arch structure
compared to the initial arch bridge, only using the arch as a bearing structure. The thesis
discovered that the Sandwich Arch BridgeвҖҷs capacities to withstand applied skew loading
increased more than ten times compared to the initial PCВӯBridge.
2.5.1
Challenging focus points
Optimal PCВӯArch configuration
The chosen configuration can influence the PCВӯArch statics and response. Relevant conВӯ
figurations to be investigated is the number and design of hinges, centerline shape, riseВӯ
toВӯspan ratio and fill height above the crown. Analytical investigations into how these
different configurations affect the sandwich bridge structure could optimise the structure.
SLВӯDeck element size
Previously used widths of SLВӯdeck elements in PCВӯArch bridges has been 1650mm+100mm
in both a fullВӯscale load test described in the article FullВӯscale load tests of PearlВӯChain
Arches[18] and Vorgod Creek Road Bridge [5], with the additional width making space for
the hammerhead joint transferring forces between adjacent PCВӯArches. Analytical invesВӯ
tigations could be done to use other SLВӯdeck configurations in a PCВӯArch in the Sandwich
Arch Bridge. The chosen SLВӯdeck element size will also affect the total PCВӯArch weight,
where reducing the weight could be beneficial. Optimising total PCВӯArch weight could
reduce the required lifting capacity, which could be found to have positive effects on conВӯ
struction time and cost.
Capacity calculations
Capacity calculations of the Sandwich Arch Bridge structure include capacities of multiple
components, PCВӯArch, fill material and top plate. The PCВӯArch is constructed using comВӯ
posite prefabricated concrete decks with different composition in each deck, resulting in
complex capacity calculations due to multiple section compositions throughout the length
of the arch. Combined elements compose the Sandwich Arch Bridge structure, a strucВӯ
ture with multiple material stiffness and elasticity, resulting in complex load response.
Calculating the components sectional properties, capacities, and load response of the
Sandwich Arch Bridge structure requires demanding analytical calculations.
PostВӯtensioning stress
In a Sandwich Arch Bridge, the resulting live loads will generally form tensile stress in the
PCВӯArch. It is of interest to find the stress from postВӯtensioning to investigate the maxiВӯ
mum compressive force applied to the arch without failure. In the article Assembly and
lifting of pearlВӯchain arches[20], the assembly and lifting of two PCВӯArches were docuВӯ
mented. The article stated that shortly after postВӯtensioning, visible cracks were formed
in the longitudinal direction. These cracks were perpendicular to the usually expected
direction of cracks in the bottom of the SLВӯdeck element from vertical loading of an arch.
The cracks illustrated the necessity for stress control in the element from applied postВӯ
tensioning force. Analytical investigations on the resulting postВӯtensioning stress in the
PCВӯArch could indicate critical positions and indicate maximum postВӯtensioning cable diВӯ
mensions so that that the PCВӯArch is affected by increased compressive stress to handle
further tensile stress.
Splitting layers
The results from the experimental study done in master thesis Development and test of
arch bridge with materials of different stiffness [19] proved that the Sandwich Arch Bridge
18
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:37==
increased capacities compared to the initial PCВӯBridge. However, the tests also revealed
flaws with the structure. One problem was splitting between the top plate and filling and
between the PCВӯArch and filling. Another uncertain aspect was the filling material, its
composition and its capabilities. It was not clear which type of filling provided the optimal
capacities needed. Analytical investigation into the response of the Sandwich Arch Bridge
structure could indicate reasons for the splitting between layers and analytical represenВӯ
tation of the splitting force could be found and used in further capacity calculations.
Bridge design competitiveness
Concluding on a bridge designs competitiveness can be done focusing on crucial aspects
seen as determinable by both developer and contractor. The Danish road administraВӯ
tion has stated that sustainability and the worlds environmental targets are a crucial part
of their strategy, focusing on reducing the CO2 in the planning, construction, operation
and maintenance phase, pushing for a green adjustment [21]. For contractors, the conВӯ
struction process, cost and time are determinable factors. Analytical investigations could
cast light on the sustainability and the construction process of the Sandwich Arch Bridge
compared to other commonly used bridge design.

rocodes with associated national annexes and the manual constitute the load and calcuВӯ
lation basis for the dimensioning of new bridges. The basis for the hierarchical structure
is illustrated in Figure 2.21.

Material Properties
Partial coefficients for materials
Partial coefficients for materials in Ultimate Limit State (ULS) for use in prefabricated conВӯ

EC2, Part 1Вӯ1, DK NA, Table 2.1 [23] as:
вҖў Concrete compression and EВӯmodulus in reinforced concrete:
Оіc = 1.40
вҖў Tensioning reinforcement strength:

2.6.2
Traffic Loads
All traffic loads are defined as Load Models and presented in EC1, part 2 [27]. The specific
Load Models to use is based on the bridge group from Load and Calculation Basis for
Bridges [22]. All new public bridges designed in Denmark is designed as Bridge Group 1.
The preВӯdefined load models is Load Model 1Вӯ4. Load Model 1 represents normal traffic
under normal conditions. Load Model 2 represents dynamic effects on smaller bridges.
Load Model 3 represents special vehicles and design cases. Load Model 4 represents
the effects of pedestrians on the bridge.
20
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:39==
2.6.3
Load combinations
Load combinations are gathered from Appendix 1 in Load and calculation basis for bridges
[22] with a basis in EC0 DK NA [28] for both Ultimate Limit State and Serviceability Limit
State. For Ultimate limit state combinations with consequence class factor, KFI, Table
A1.2 in EC0 is used with the value for KFI listed.
2.6.4
Ultimate Limit State (ULS) calculations
ULS calculations are computed based on EC2, part 2, section 6 [27] in regards to:
вҖў Bending moment capacity with and without axial force
вҖў Shear force capacity
вҖў Compressive and tensile capacities
The ULS calculations should prove the ability of the structure to withstand all relevant
applied loads without failure or collapse.
2.6.5
Serviceability Limit State (SLS) calculations
SLS covers following limit states as given in EC2, part 2, section 7.1 [29]:
вҖў Stress limitation
вҖў Crack control
вҖў Deflection control
Compressive and tensile stress should be limited under characteristic load combination
to avoid longitudinal and transverse cracks. Cracking under characteristic loading may
lead to a reduction in durability. Compressive stress should be limited to k1fck with the
recommended value of k1 given in EC2, part 2, section 7.2 as 0.6 [29]. Tensile stress
should be limited to the concrete tensile force fctm.
Complying with the stress limitation, where the concrete stress is within limits, no crack
control is required. Deflection of the arch under characteristic loading is assumed to be
within limits, and further calculations are outside the scope of this thesis.
2.6.6
PreВӯ and PostВӯtensioning
Necessary space between both preВӯ and postВӯtensioning strands is limited to the minimum
clear space between preВӯtensioning tendons given in EC2, part 1Вӯ1, section 8, вүҘ2 П• [24].
Necessary clear coverage for bridges with relevant exposure and environmental class is
given in EC2, part 2, DK NA as 50mm for reinforcement and tensioning strands. Since the
strands in the cable duct are 60mm, all values include +5 mm for normal/critical control
class [30].
Tensioning strands data used in preВӯ and postВӯtensioning is gathered from manufacturer
Freyssinet PrestressingвҖҷs European Technical Approval (ETA) [31]. The postВӯtensioning
used in the PCВӯArch calculations are bonded internal tensioning based on the use of lubriВӯ
cated, uncoated strands in a corrugated metal sheet injected with cement grout after tenВӯ
sioning of the strands with a C range anchor. PostВӯtensioning cables are made up of T15
(П• 15.7mm) strands with a characteristic ultimate strength of 1860 MPa. The friction coВӯ
efficient for lubricated bonded strand in metal sheet, Вө, is 0.17 with Angular displacement
factor, k, of 0.007 rad/m. Initial anchor slip for postВӯtensioning strands, вҲҶsl, is 8 mm for
T15 strands. Maximum relaxation is given to be вүӨ2.5% resulting in relaxation class 2 after
EC2, part 1Вӯ1, section 3.3.2, with an relaxation loss at thousand hours, ПҒ1000 of 2.5 [24].
The postВӯtension cable is stressed by maximum force Fp.max =min{k1 В· Fpk; k2 В· Fp0.1k}
with k1 = 0.8 and k2 = 0.9 as prescribed in EC2, part 1Вӯ1, section 5.10.2 [24] and the
Sandwich Arches
21
==PAGE:40==
guaranteed breaking load, Fpk, and elastic limit, Fp0.1k, given in producers ETA. The maxВӯ
imum postВӯtensioning force, Pmax, is found by multiplying the maximum force Fp.max by
the number of strands in the cable, np.s. The postВӯtensioning is assumed to be conducted
from both sides of the PCВӯArch.
The designing force being applied to the concrete from postВӯtensioning is found by subВӯ
tracting the immediate losses from the maximum postВӯtensioning force, Pmax. The folВӯ
lowing immediate influences are to be considered when calculating the final postВӯtension
force, as described in EC2 part 1Вӯ1 5.10 [24]:
вҖў Friction вҲҶPВө(x)
вҖў Anchorage slip вҲҶPsl
вҖў Elastic deformation of concrete вҲҶPel
The postВӯtensioning of the PCВӯArch is assumed to be done after 28days resulting in no
long term loss of tensioning force relating to creep and shrinkage. The only long term
postВӯtensioning force loss considered is the long term relaxation of the steel tendons.

 Sandwich Arch Bridge

Chapter 7:
Bridge Design Comparison
The Sandwich Arch Bridge is a filled spandrel arch bridge where the chosen design of the
PCВӯArch can have a significant influence on the response. The arch centre line shape,
hinge configuration, riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio, the height of the fill layer above the crown and the
SLВӯdeck element size are all choices that will affect the capacity and cost of the PCВӯArch
differently. It is of interest to find a combination of effects to optimise the Sandwich Arch
Bridge structures capacity and cost. All calculations relating to this chapter can be seen
in Appendix A.
3.1
Hinge configuration
As described in the arch static, the hinge configuration of the arch influences the overall
static system. For an arch to be categorised as threeВӯhinged, a hinge at the arch crown
needs to be evident. For PCВӯArches, concrete hinges seem to be the obvious choice. For
concrete hinges, we can expect some rotational resistance which could influence the reВӯ
sponse of the structure. A study done on concrete hinges at DTU in 2014 [32] investigated
the Freyssinet and Mesnager hinge, which is widely tested and have been implemented
in concrete structures. The hinge is made by reducing the section height, creating a hinge
throat with possible cross reinforcement in the reduced section, as illustrated in Figure 3.1.
b2
b1
Hinge Throat
Cross Reinforcement
Hinge Body
Figure 3.1: Concrete Hinge
Sandwich Arches
23
==PAGE:42==
The test used prior research results to optimise the test setup for concrete hinge testing,
considering the hinge application in an arch structure, applying a constant normal force
while increasing the moment in the hinge throat until failure. Testing was done for 16 hinge
specimen with different ratios between b1 and b2. The test result indicated, as expected,
that a large ratio between b1 and b2 will result in a higher rotational stiffness and a larger
fracture moment. Looking at the results within the elastic region of hinge response where
no permanent deformations will develop, the hinge rotation was 7 to 10% of the rotation
at full capacity. The test result indicated that the hinge is not suited for arch construction
with large deformations [32].
The casting of the hinge, taking into account the cross reinforcement and postВӯtension
duct, could be a complicated process. PostВӯtensioning through the hinge will also increase
the rotation resistance of the hinge. The arch would need to be split up in two halfвҖҷs
and postВӯtensioned before the hinge is cast. Installation of the arch using the described
method earlier, lifting the arch into position, would also need to consider not to affect
the hinge with large deformation, which could inflict permanent deformations in the hinge
region. For arches with a small riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio, a slight decrease in the rise will cause
a significant increase in the horizontal reaction at supports. For a threeВӯhinged arch,
the resulting increase in horizontal reaction force can not be taken as increased bending
moments. For PCВӯArch structures, the effect of a three hinges arch is not evident for
concrete hinges behaving within the elastic regime when the response is between an
ideal hinge and a fixed hinge. The top deck and fill material layer will prevent rotation in
the hinge if the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept is assumed.
The connection between the arch and supports at the abutments is constructed using an
orthogonal saddle bearing hinge. The end elements of the PCВӯArch is placed with the end
surface aligned to the abutment with the space between the end element and abutment
filled with mortar as illustrated in Figure 3.2.
Abutment
Mortar
PC-Arch
t
Figure 3.2: Orthogonal saddle bearing hinge
The hinge was developed to be implemented in a fullВӯscale load test of a 13m span PCВӯ
Arch [18]. The behaviour of the orthogonal saddle bearing hinge was determined based
on response data from the test. The influence on the response from different thickness of
the mortar joint, t, and the YoungвҖҷs modulus, E, was investigated. The test indicated that
the hinge performs as practically fixed in the elastic region. However, the level of rotation
can, to some extent, be altered with the mortar thickness and YoungвҖҷs modulus. Beyond
the elastic limit, cracking occurs, and the hinge performance is closer to an ideal hinge
[5].
The Sandwich Arch Bridge is implemented as a twoВӯhinged system using orthogonal sadВӯ
dle bearing hinges at the abutments. The low riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio, lifting and installation
24
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:43==
are all factors favouring the twoВӯhinged system. Although the saddle bearing hinge acted
as practically fixed in the elastic region, the supports are assumed to be hinged for this
thesis.
3.2
Arch center line shape
The different arch shapes illustrated in Figure 2.8Нҫ Circular, Parabolic, Catenary and EllipВӯ
tical have different shapes with different inclinations along the length of the arch. ConsidВӯ
ering the prefabrication of the SLВӯdecks elements, the circular shape would be favourable
due to the elementsвҖҷ equal design. Equal design of each SLВӯdeck element will result in
reduced production cost, easier transportation and assembly. Opposing sides with a cirВӯ
cular arch shape is the increased moment and horizontal force from loading. For both
circular, parabolic and catenary shape, the distribution of the load from fill material will be
close to similar. In contrast, the fill layerвҖҷs height will be reduced over the whole length
for the elliptical shape. Evaluating the responses given in the introduction for the different
arch centre lines, the elliptical depicts a higher moment. The elliptical shape is eliminated
based on the high bending moment, complex element production and erection procedure.
The fill material between the top plate and the arch will be an unevenly distributed force on
the arch. To illustrate the effect of the fill material on a circular, parabolic and catenary twoВӯ
hinged arch, the bending moment response is calculated using a fill material of pervious
concrete with a selfВӯweight of 19kN/m, which have been used as fill material in previous
PCВӯArch constructions [25]. The response can be seen in Figure 3.3 for a riseВӯtoВӯspan
ratio of
1
10 with fill height not above the crown. The different arch centre line shapesвҖҷ reВӯ
sponse is close to similar, with the circular shape depicting the smallest bending moments.
The response from different live loads illustrated in Figure 2.12 to Figure 2.14 for circular,
parabolic and catenary shape depicts negligible difference for low riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios and
hinge configuration. The preferable centre line shape for a PCВӯArch to be implemented
in a Sandwich Arch Bridge is seen to be circular.
Figure 3.3: Bending moment distribution from fill material
3.3
RiseВӯtoВӯspan ratio
To illustrate the effect of the riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio on the relation between max bending moВӯ
ment and horizontal reaction, both have been calculated using riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios of 1/5,
1/8, 1/10, 1/12 and 1/14. The calculations are done for a circular twoВӯhinged arch with
Sandwich Arches
25
==PAGE:44==
span L=30m, loaded only from selfВӯweight load from the arch, filling material and top deck
pr.m dept. The selfВӯweight from the arch and top deck is calculated as a uniformly disВӯ
tributed load, while the selfВӯweight from the fill material is calculated as a nonВӯsymmetrical
load, decreasing and increasing along the span. The resulting maximum bending moment
along with the resulting horizontal reaction force can be seen in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Horizontal reaction Вӯ Max Bending moment
The maximum bending moment can be seen to decrease with lower riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios,
while the horizontal reaction force increases with a lower ratio. In between riseВӯtoВӯspan
ratios, the rate of increase of horizontal reaction and decrease of the max bending moment
can be seen to decrease with lower ratios. The percentage changes for maximum moment
and horizontal reaction force between different spanВӯtoВӯrise ratios are listed in Table 3.1. It
can be seen that for a ratio lower than 1/10, the percentage increase in horizontal reaction
force is lower than the percentage decrease in bending moment, which is favourable.

The resulting horizontal reaction force is often dimensional due to the abutmentsвҖҷ necesВӯ
sary strength to handle the force. If the bridge is placed on rock formations or reinforced
concrete foundations, it is beneficial with a lower riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio where the loads are
carried as compression in the arch rather than in bending. From the analysis of the selfВӯ
weight loads, it is seen that the percentage decrease of the maximum bending moment
is smaller than the percentage increase of horizontal reaction force from riseВӯtoВӯspan raВӯ
tios below 1/10. LiveВӯloads applied in further dimensioning processes will likely increase
both bending moment and horizontal reaction force. The moment from nonВӯsymmetrical
loads is often the dimensioning load causing increased bending moment over horizontal
reaction force, calling for a lower riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio. Eventually, increased fill layer height
above the crown will also affect loading, causing an increased need for compressive caВӯ
pacity.
In the journal article Precast PearlВӯChain concrete arch bridges concerning a design exВӯ
ample case study for a 30m span PCВӯArch using SLВӯdecks was conducted to find the caВӯ
pacity of the arch with different SLВӯdeck crossВӯsections, riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios, level of backВӯ
filling, and the number of postВӯtensioning cables [17]. The study found that the PCВӯArchвҖҷs
positive bending moment capacity was greater in a bridge with a riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio 1/13
than 1/7.5, with the difference increasing for increased SLВӯdeck crossВӯsection heights.
From the discussed effects of the riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio on both loading and response, a ratio
in the range of 1/10 to 1/14 is seen to be favourable for a PCВӯArch to be used in a Sandwich
Arch Bridge.
Sandwich Arches

==PAGE:46==
3.4
Fill height
The height of the fill material above the crown will influence the load on the PCВӯArch
from fill material and the distance between the arch and the top deck along the span. To
illustrate the effect of fill material height on the response, the resulting moment load and
horizontal reaction force have been calculated with fill material height above crown equal
to 0m, 1m, 2, and 3m for riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios of 1/10 and 1/14. The calculations are done
for a circular twoВӯhinged bridge with span L=30m affected only from selfВӯweight load from
the arch, filling material and top deck pr.m dept.
The resulting moment from the selfВӯweight load is given in Figure 3.6 where the response
for riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio 1/10 is given as solid lines and 1/14 as dotted lines. The maximum
and minimum moment for ratio 1/14 is lower than for 1/10 as expected after the investigaВӯ
tion for riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio effects. The increase of the fill material height above the crown
can be seen to reduce the maximum and minimum moment for each meter increase of fill
material height for both ratios. The figure shows that the resulting maximum and minimum
moment for a 1/10 ratio with 3m fill above the crown is less than a 1/14 ratio with no fill
above the crown. The fill material above the crown will act as a uniformly distributed load
reducing the moment caused by the nonВӯsymmetrical load caused by the fill material on
both sides of the crown.
Figure 3.6: Bending moment from different fill height above crown
To further illustrate the effects of increased fill height above the crown of the arch, the
resulting bending moment is plotted along with the resulting horizontal reaction force in
Figure 3.7. The plot illustrates that the reduction in bending moment from increased fill
above the crown decreases for lower riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio. The horizontal reaction forces
for a rise to span ratio of 1/14 is also seen to be higher than 1/10, as expected when the
volume of fill material is more immense for decreased riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio, and horizontal
force is higher for lower riseВӯtoВӯspan ratios. Table 3.3 are listing the percentage decrease
of bending moment and increase of horizontal reaction force between each fill height
above crown value. The table shows that the percentage increase in horizontal reaction
force is higher than the decrease in bending moment for all fill heights for both riseВӯtoВӯspan
ratios. This could indicate that the positive sides with the reduction of bending moment
due to increased fill height is reduced due to the resulting high increase in horizontal
reaction force.

The influence from fill height above the crown on PCВӯArchesвҖҷ moment capacity was inВӯ
vestigated in the journal article Precast PearlВӯChain concrete arch bridges. The study
investigated the capacity using different SLВӯdeck thickness with different number of postВӯ
tension cables and height of backfilling, 0m, 1m and 2m. The study found that the positive
moment capacity increased when increasing the height of fill above the crown. In contrast,
the negative moment capacity increased when increasing the height from 0m to 1m but
decreased with a height above 1m [17]. For preliminary capacity calculations, it is seen
as conservative to use 0m of fill above the crown. Optimisation of the bending moment
distribution can be done by increasing the fill height. However, increased fill above the
crown will increase the horizontal reaction force, total weight, cost, environmental impact
and installation time of the Sandwich Arch Bridge.
3.5
Element size
The size of the SLВӯdeck elements can be chosen to optimise the production, transportaВӯ
tion and installation of the Sandwich Arch Bridge affecting the cost and environmental
impact. The size will also affect the PCВӯArchвҖҷs capacity before the fill material and the top
deck is installed. Previously used widths of SLВӯdeck elements in PCВӯArch bridges has
been 1650mm+100mm, with the additional width making space for the hammerhead joint
transferring forces between adjacent PCВӯArches. The SLВӯdeck elements could be manВӯ
ufactured with any desired width. However, an investigation is done of the already used
1650mm compared to the standard production width 2400mm and a smaller 1000mm
wide to look into differences and possible benefits.
Sandwich Arches
29
==PAGE:48==
3.5.1
Material distribution
The SLВӯdeck section heights used in the fullВӯscale load test was 220mm. This height
did not have the necessary reinforcement coverage for use in bridge structures with the
relevant exposure and environmental class as described subsection 2.6.6. Construction
of the Vorgod creek road bridge was done using a section height of 270mm for the SLВӯ
deck element. The number of LAC blocks varies with increased SLВӯdeck width, with 2
LAC blocks in the 1000mm wide deck, 4 LAC blocks in the 1650mm wide deck and 6 LAC
blocks in the 2400mm wide deck. Increased capacities could be found using a section
height of 320mm, 370mm or 420mm, where the LAC blocksвҖҷ height remains the same,
but the height above, hc, increases. In the longitudinal direction, investigations are done
of element lengths 1600mm, 2200mm, 2800mm and 3400mm with 2 to 5 LAC blocks.
Using the three different widths and the four different section heights, the percentage
of LAC in the transverse section is calculated, as seen in Table 3.4. The percentage
LAC area is seen to be similar for PC 1650 and PC 2400. The PC 1000 has less LAC
section percentage, illustrating a less optimised section than PC 1650 and PC 2400. All
showed a reduced percentage for increased section height. The percentage of LAC in
the longitudinal section is calculated and is seen in Table 3.5. Longer element lengths
are seen to have a more optimised section and need fewer joints, which reduces the
use of strong concrete. All element lengths showed a reduced percentage for increased
section height. Increased LAC percentage in both transverseВӯ and longitudinal section
length will result in a lighter PCВӯArch, preferable considering the lifting of the PCВӯArch at
the construction site.

The ideal crossВӯsection area, Ai, for each section is given by the effective total area,
Atot.e, found by multiplying the effective section width, bSL.e, with the section height, hSL,

element. The NA for concrete, zib.C, and joint section, zib.M, is found to be at the middle
of the section. For the SL sections, the NA is found to be above the middle of the section.

zib.e=170mm, 35mm above the middle of the section.

3.5.3
PostВӯtensioning stress
The maximum postВӯtension cable size depends on the following variables:
вҖў Clear space for the postВӯtensioning cable duct in crossВӯsection.
вҖў Minimum concrete coverage for the postВӯtensioning cable duct.
вҖў Length of the SLВӯdeck element.
вҖў Sectional size of the abutment element at support for anchoring.
вҖў Internal stresses in PCВӯArch.
At the ends of the SLВӯdeck elements, the postВӯtensioning cable duct is positioned in the
crossВӯsectionвҖҷs centre. The maximum cable to comply with the necessary clear space
of the section can be altered by removing the preВӯtensioning strands under the postВӯ
tensioning duct. Anchoring of the postВӯtensioning cable is limited by the length of the
abutment element and the section height of the abutment element on the anchoring end.
These values can be altered to comply with all relevant postВӯtensioning cables available
from
producer,

The postВӯtension cable is positioned in a curve in each SLВӯdeck element so that the postВӯ
tensioning cable follows the preferred centerline shape in the PCВӯArch. In a circular shape
arch, each SLВӯdeck will have the same position of the postВӯtensioning cable, being at the
crossВӯsection centre at the ends and increasing to a top point at the middle of the element.
The height of the cable above the crossВӯsection centre, the cable rise fc, is dependent
on the length of the element, Le, the total length of the span, L, and the rise of the arch,
f. Smaller element lengths will, in general, have less rise of the cable in the element.
The rise of the cable duct in the element is limited by the minimum concrete coverage of
postВӯtensioning cable ducts of 60mm as described in subsection 2.6.6.
The internal stresses developed when postВӯtensioning the PCВӯArch can be a dimensional
factor for maximum allowable cables. The stresses are calculated using the section propВӯ
erties, ideal crossВӯsection area Ai and second moment of inertia for concrete in the top
Wi.t and bottom Wi.b of the section. The crossВӯsection properties were found using a calВӯ

and two SL sections with min and max cable rise fc.

The critical section was found to be the LAC section close to the end of the element. The
NA in the LAC section is positioned above the postВӯtensioning cable position, increasing
the compression at the bottom of the section. The increased compression force combined
with a reduced second moment of area for the bottom section results in a high stress
concentration. At the middle of the deck, the increased cable rise will reduce the moment
arm between the postВӯtensioning force and the NA resulting in reduced concrete stress.
The maximum cable size is found by controlling the concrete stress at the bottom of the
critical LAC section from the postВӯtensioning force. For the PC1650 Вӯ SL270 arch, 13C15
is the maximum cable size complying with the compressive stress limitation as seen in

The rise of the cable in the SLВӯdeck element will also affect the PCВӯArchвҖҷs bending moВӯ
ment capacities. The postВӯtensioning of the SLВӯelements will increase both positive and
negative bending moment capacities. At the centre of the crossВӯsection, the position at
the ends of the SLВӯdeck elements will increase the positive capacity more than the negВӯ
ative. However, in the middle of the SLВӯdeck element, where the postВӯtensioning cable
is positioned higher than the centre, the opposite effect will cause a higher increase in
negative moment capacity over positive moment capacity.
3.6
PCВӯArch weight
The SLВӯdeck elementвҖҷs width will affect the PCВӯArchвҖҷs total weight, affecting the necessary
lifting equipment to position the PCВӯArch at construction site. The PCВӯArch is implemented
as twoВӯhinged resulting in the construction and lifting of the whole arch. Calculating the
total weight is done using the relevant material densities for concrete, LAC and steel for
the PCВӯArch, including postВӯtension cable and additional concrete for transverse joints.
Assumptions and simplifications have been taken when calculating the necessary lifting
height and vertical distance when using a mobile crane. The vertical lifting distance is asВӯ
sumed to be the same as the arch bridge span, L. An example is illustrated in Figure 3.14
for an arch span of L=15m. From the figure, it is clear that all PCВӯArches can be lifted
and positioned within a lifting radius of 15m.
15m
15m
Figure 3.14: Vertical lifting distance example
The necessary height of the mobile crane lifting hook is composed of the assumed necВӯ
essary connection height and lifting height. The connection height is calculated from the
Sandwich Arches
37
==PAGE:56==
span of the bridge L. The assumed value, L/5, is chosen so that the lifting cables will
have space to create equal tension in each lifting cable, or of not a lifting beam is used,
the incline of the lifting cable is not causing increased horizontal forces. The necessary
lifting height is assumed to be determined by the necessary clear space under bridges,
hfree, as described in the manual вҖқGrundlag for udformning af trafikarealerвҖқ (Basis for deВӯ
sign of traffic areas) chapter 6.3.3 as hfree = 4.63m [34], multiplied by an assumed factor
of 1.2. The lifting height can be calculated for each span of L using Equation 3.18. The
value is given as an estimation and could be optimised for each project.

the PCВӯArch construction site, besides the road, and the final installation site. Using a
mobile crane, the craneвҖҷs position could be close to the optimal lifting position, either on
the road under the archвҖҷs final position or the road that will go over the final bridge. The
resulting total weight, calculated in a simplified way assuming LAC blocks throughout the
whole longitudinal length, along with the calculated capacities for relevant mobile cranes,
is plotted in Figure 3.16. The lifting capacities for the different mobile cranes is found using
the online lifting diagrams from BMS [35] [36] [37] [38] using the calculated lifting height
and vertical distance. Where the capacity line is straight, the lifting capacity is higher, so
capacity at the inclination point is used. The figure will not be used for conclusive design
but will estimate the necessary crane for each PCВӯArch configuration in the preliminary
design.

An optimal SLВӯdeck element size in a PCВӯArch has to be seen in combination with the
arch span. The load on the arch will increase the forces acting on the arch with increased
span demanding higher capacities justifying an increased section height. For increased
section heights, the elementвҖҷs width and length should be in the top end to optimise the
LAC percentage, reducing the overall weight. Increased element sizes will reduce the
production, transportation and installation time, also reducing cost. Total PCВӯArch weight
should be below mobile crane lifting capacities to reduce the installation cost and total
time.

4.1
General Concept
Compression
Tension
Shear
Load
Figure 4.1: Sandwich Arch Bridge concept
The Sandwich Arch Bridge Concept is based on force couples in the top and bottom of the
crossВӯsection with an arm between them that can transfer shear. It is the same concept
used when designing IВӯbeams to resist bending moment. The top and bottom flange make
up the compression and tension zones, and the web transfers shear between the flanges.
In sandwich arches, the top plate and the bottom arch take up the couple forces, while
the filling material transfers shear between them. This means that the Sandwich Arch
BridgeвҖҷs bending moment capacity depends on the top plate and bottom arch capacity
to take up horizontal forces and the vertical distance between the two members at any
point in the bridge provided by the height of the filling material. The shear force capacity
is based on the crossВӯsectional area of the filling layer and the shear stress capacity of
the filling material.
Sandwich Arches
41
==PAGE:60==
The sandwich arch concept will first be relevant when the bridge is finished, the concrete
is cured, and all components work together. This means that the dead loads of the bridge
will not be carried by the whole sandwich arch structure, but rather the bottom arch inВӯ
dividually, since the dead loads are present before the concrete is cured and the bridge
components work together. Once the sandwich arch is completed and the sandwich arch
response concept works, the whole bridge will carry the traffic loads.
4.2
Erection Procedure
The erection procedure of a PCВӯbridge is described and tested on two previous occasionsНҫ
during the project resulting in the article Assembly and Lifting of PearlВӯChain Arches [20]
the procedure for the PCВӯArch was described and competed. This was done during the
erection of the first PCВӯbridge, built in 2015 [5][25]. The main parts of the erection proceВӯ
dure are illustrated in Figure 4.2.
PC-ARCH MANUFACTURING
Joints between PC-arch elements
are casted and post-tensioning
are done after joint hardening.
PC-ARCH LIFTING
The PC-arches are rotated in air
and lifted from post-tensioning
location to final location.
JOINT CASTING
Scaffolding and casting of joint at
abutments and hammerhead
joints between PC-arches.
POURING OF FILL MATERIAL
Pouring and compacting of fill
material in sequences from each
side of the PC-arch.
TOP PLATE
Scaffolding, casting and
post-tensioning of top plate.
BRIDGE COMPLETION
Wearing course and traffic safety
rails are constructed and bridge
ready for use.
Figure 4.2: Erection procedure description
During the erection process, the PCВӯArch will be affected by different loading situations
requiring capacity control. Listed below are the relevant cases:
Case 1: PostВӯtensioning of PCВӯArch
After the joints between PCВӯArch members
have cured, postВӯtensioning are done apВӯ
plying compressive stress to the PCВӯArch.
The compressive stress is to be controlled
against SLS limits.
Figure 4.3: PostВӯtensioning of PCВӯArch

Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:61==
Case 2: Lifting of PCВӯArch
When lifting the PCВӯArch, bending moment
and normal force from arch selfВӯweight will
cause forces in the arch. The number and
position of lifting points along the length of
the arch can be altered to handle forces.
The lifting point should also be positioned so
that the deformations cause the arch span
to reduce slightly for easier positioning at
the supports. It is assumed that a beam or
a truss system can be used so that the liftВӯ
ing wires are vertical, only affecting the PCВӯ
Arch with vertical forces. SLS and ULS conВӯ
trol.
Figure 4.4: Lifting of PCВӯArch
Case 3: Sequential filling
Fill material is poured and compacted in layВӯ
ers on top of positioned PCВӯArches on each
side causing uneven loading from the fill
material on the arch. ULS and SLS control.
Figure 4.5: Sequential filling
Case 4: Total deadВӯload
Total deadВӯload include loads from fill maВӯ
terial on the sides and above crown and
the top plate. The fill material on each side
will cause increased bending moment in the
arch, while fill above crown and the top plate
will affect the arch as a UDL causing inВӯ
creased concrete compression.
ULS and
SLS control.
Figure 4.6: Total deadВӯload
All loads applied after the fill material and top plate have cured will be taken by both the
PCВӯArch and the top plate working as a sandwich structure.
4.3
Design
Loads, response, and capacities have been calculated for six different Sandwich Arch
Bridges. The initial varying parameters are the span lengths and element widths to invesВӯ
tigate the development of these factors. The span lengths, L, are 27 m, 35 m and 50 m,
and the element widths, bSL.a, are 1750 mm, similar to what has been used in PCВӯArches
before, and 2500 mm, similar to the original production width of the SLВӯdeck, 2400mm,
with additional hammerhead joint width. The rise of the bottom arch is also varying reВӯ

5.1
Loads
Sandwich Arch Bridges are subject to various types of loads throughout their lifetime. The
dead loads change during construction until the bridge is erected. Then live loads are inВӯ
troduced to the structure. Live loads are defined by load models based on the size and
usage of the bridge. The arch structure can take up uniformly distributed loads as comВӯ
pression through the arch, which leads to improved resistance towards bending moment
in the crossВӯsection. This means that the bridge cannot only be designed depending on
the most significant applied load but also the position and distribution of the loads. DeВӯ
tailed calculations of loads and load combinations is presented in Appendix B.
5.1.1
Dead Loads
The dead load consists of the selfВӯweight of all permanent components of the Sandwich
Arch Bridge and changes along with the construction phases of the structure. The main
components are the PCВӯArch, pervious concrete filling and the top deck. Since compresВӯ
sion in the arch increases when subject to uniformly distributed loads, resistance towards
bending moment is improved. Therefore, the arch will get increased capabilities once
the bridge is finished and the above structure provides UDL to the arch. Therefore, it is
essential to design for the most critical dead load cases during the bridgeвҖҷs construction.
It should also be checked for the selfВӯweight of the arch when the arch is lifted in place.
This investigation should avoid problems with significant negative bending moments. AnВӯ
other aspect of the dead load related to arch bridges is that the structure becomes thinner
towards the bridgeвҖҷs centre. This means that the dead load gets more prominent towards
the sides and leads to uneven loading, which is unfavourable.
The dead load is found by calculating the individual dead loads from the main bridge comВӯ
ponents and combining them to one distributed load. Therefore, the calculation procedure
is divided up into four procedures respectively:
Sandwich Arches
47
==PAGE:66==
1. SelfВӯweight of the PCВӯArch
2. SelfВӯweight of the filling
3. SelfВӯweight of the top deck
4. Combined selfВӯweight of the entire structure
All dead loads are computed related to the actual width of the SLВӯdecks used in the pearlВӯ
chain arch.
PearlВӯchain Arch
The selfВӯweight of the PCВӯArch is found using a simplified method where the final value
ends up being a uniformly distributed line load per meter span. The actual weight of the
arch, dependent on the span, would be heavier towards the ends of the arch. However,
since the riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio is relatively low, the difference between the actual and simpliВӯ
fied value is marginal.
Equation 5.1 presents the function used to find the dead load of the PCВӯArch. This equaВӯ
tion is based on the dead load of the arch per meter arch length, gA.A, the arch length,
LA, and the span of the bridge, L. The weight of the PCВӯArch per meter arch length is
given by Equation 5.2. In this equation, the numerator is divided into three parts. The
first part relates to the selfВӯweight of the sections, which includes LAC blocks, the secВӯ
ond part relates to the sections without LAC blocks, and the third part considers the dead
load of the reinforcement in the arch. The length of the arch is found by using EquaВӯ
tion 5.3, which depends on the derivative of the arch function, yc(x). Equation 5.4 gives
the ratio between the length of the element with LAC blocks and the total element length,
while Equation 5.5 gives the ratio between the length without LAC blocks and total length.
Figure 5.1 visualises the characteristic dead load of the arch as a graph.

Pervious Concrete Filling during Sequential Filling
The procedure of filling the pervious concrete over the pearlВӯchain arch is an important
focus area for the designing loads of the bridge. This procedure leads to skew loading in
sequential steps until the filling reaches the crown of the arch. The pervious concrete has
water draining capabilities, but this capability is reduced if the filling gets too compact. It is
proven that the layers of pervious concrete during the filling procedure should not exceed
270 mm [25] to reduce the risk of a too compact end result. The pervious concrete filling
load under sequential filling is therefore put to the skew loading that occurs when the filling
has reached the top of the crown on one side while the filling on the other side has one less
layer of filling. This load gives the highest bending moments during the procedure since
the highest skew loading comes from the last layer, which has the highest selfВӯweight of
all layers underneath. All subsequent layers being filled after this layer will give uniform
load contributions, favourable for the pearlВӯchain arch.
The various spans evaluated in this report will have varying rises of the arch since the
riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio is chosen to be close to 1/13. This leads to different numbers of filling
layers for each span. The number of filling layers only refers to the layers of filling below
the crown since this is the most interesting number related to the skew filling load. The
number of filling layers related to each span is presented in Table 5.1.
Table 5.1: Number of filling layers below crown

Total Pervious Concrete Filling
The total dead load of the pervious concrete filling layer is defined by Equation 5.8. Here,
the density of the concrete ПҒpc is multiplied by the height of the filling, hf (x), and the
actual width of the SLВӯdecks, bSL.a. The total height of the filling varies dependent on the
span. It is calculated by adding the rise, f, with the height of filling above the crown, hf.top,
and subtracting the height of the arch dependent on x, yc (x), presented in Equation 5.9.
Figure 5.3 shows the total filling dead load as a graph.

Concrete Top Plate
The dead load of the top plate is constant over the entire span of the Sandwich Arch
Bridge since the geometry does not change. Equation 5.10 gives the dead load of the
top plate. The equation is dependent on the density of concrete, ПҒc, multiplied by the
crossВӯsectional area of the concrete, Ac.TP , added together with the density of steel, ПҒs,
multiplied by the crossВӯsectional area of the postВӯtensioning strand located in the top plate,
Ap.post.TP . Figure 5.4 presents the final dead load of the top plate.
gTP = ПҒc В· Ac.TP + ПҒs В· Ap.post.TP
(5.10)
Figure 5.4: Dead load of top plate with 27 m span and 1.75 m element width
Total Dead Load
The equation representing the total dead load of the entire structure is found by combining
the equations of the dead load of the pearlВӯchain arch, gA.A (x), top plate, gTP , and the total
filling, gf (x). This is presented in Equation 5.11. The total load defined by Equation 5.11
relative to the span of 27 m and the element width of 1.75 m is presented in Figure 5.5.
g (x) = gA.S (x) + gTP + gf (x)

5.1.2
Live Loads
Applied live loads depend on the usage of the bridge. In Denmark, the usage category
is defined using bridge groups defined in Load and Calculation Basis for Bridges [22].
Bridge Group I refers to all new public bridges being designed for regular traffic and should
be used when finding the designing live loads of this report. EC1, part 2 [27] defines
the general live loads of various bridge types as load models. Axle loads with varying
magnitudes define each load model. Some of the load models also have UDL working
together with the axle loads.
The predefined load models are referred to as load model 1, 2, 3 and 4. Load Model
2 considers the dynamic effects of traffic load on smaller bridges and is often the preВӯ
dominant load model for spans between 3Вӯ7 m. Load Model 4 represents the effect of
pedestrians on the bridge. This will not be the designing live load for bridges designed to
withstand traffic loads from vehicles.
Load model 1 covers most of the effects from lorries and cars on bridges under normal
conditions. Load model 3 concerns special vehicles on the bridge. Load model 1 and 3
will be checked for when finding the designing live load of the bridge.
Load Model 1
Load model 1 (LM1) is based on the distribution and magnitude of forces showed in FigВӯ
ure 5.6. The model consists of two axle loads per driving lane divided by two wheels per
axle load. The location of the designed SLВӯdeck is chosen to be furthest out to one of the
bridge sides where there is only one neighbouring element. Since the bridge is designed
per element width of the SLВӯdecks, the maximum loaded width will be 2.5 m. This means
that the loaded width will never exceed the width of the first lane, and only loads relevant
to this lane will be used.
52
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:71==
Figure 5.6: Application of load model 1 [27]
The calculations of the respective characteristic axle load and UDL is presented in EquaВӯ
tion 5.12 and Equation 5.13. The characteristic axle loads and UDL is presented in FigВӯ
ure 5.6. The adjustment factors, ОұQ1 = 1.0 and Оұq1 = 0.67, related to bridge group I, is
defined in the Danish national annex to EC1, part 2 under section 4.3.2 (3), NOTES 1
and 2 [39]. The factor, kaxle, related to Equation 5.12 is dependent on the element width,
bSL.a. If the width is 1.75 m, the bridge section will only be loaded by one wheel per axle
load, which results in the axle load being reduced by a factor, kaxle, of 0.5. The same
factor becomes 1.0 when looking at the width of 2.5 m. Equation 5.13 is multiplied by the
element width, bSL.a so that the UDL is defined as a line load in further calculations.
Q1 = ОұQ1 В· Q1k В· kaxle
(5.12)
q1 = Оұq1 В· q1k В· bSL.a
(5.13)
Load Model 3
Load model 3 (LM3) represents loads from special vehicles on the bridge. Since the
bridge is under Bridge Group I, it should be designed for the load model from bridge
class 150 from Annex A of DS/EN 1991Вӯ2 DK NA[40]. This load model is presented in
Figure 5.7 where all distances between loads are given in meters and all load magnitudes
in kilonewton. Annex A also defines a shock factor, ПҶ, of 1.25 for bridges under 50 m,
which should be multiplied with each axle load. LM3 can also be combined with the UDL
from LM1, but the UDL should not be placed closer than 10 m from the nearest axle load
of LM3.
