5.2
Load Combinations
Load combinations are computed to find the most critical cases leading to the highest
internal forces in the different structural elements of the sandwich structure. The relevant
loads and related load combinations change during the erection of the bridge when the
sandwich structure is complete and after when live loads are introduced. All load comВӯ
binations and related load factors are taken from Load and Calculation Basis for Bridges
[22].
5.2.1
Load Model Comparison
To reduce the number of necessary load combinations, the internal forces from the charВӯ
acteristic values of LM1 and LM3 are analysed and compared. This is to see if one of the
models can be neglected in future calculations. The complete calculation is presented in
Appendix C.
Figure 5.8: Application of Load Models on bridge with 27 m span
Figure 5.8 presents the various applications of LM1 and LM3 on the 27 m span sandВӯ
wich bridge. LM3 is mirrored relative to the distribution presented in subsubsection 5.1.2
to get the higher bending moments to appear on the left side of the bridge, where the
higher values of LM1 is located. LM1 and LM3 are applied at midВӯspan to get the highВӯ
est relative bending moments from the load models. The application of the load models
at quarterВӯpoint is to get the highest relative negative bending moments in the opposite
54
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:73==
quarterВӯpoints. Lastly, LM1 is applied close to the support to get the theoretically highВӯ
est shear forces. These forces will, in practice, get distributed more evenly down in the
filling and spread over a larger surface. The forces may also be partially distributed to
the foundations laying next to the filling. The investigation of shear forces is therefore
based on conservative assumptions. If LM3 is moved closer to the support relative to the
distribution presented when applied at the quarterВӯpoint, the axle loads will start to move
off the bridge, and the internal forces of the bridge will be reduced. Therefore, this last
distribution of LM3 is neglected.
What is seen from the combined bending moment diagram presented in Figure 5.9 is that
LM1 solely defines the maximum bending moments at any point of the bridge. The same
applies to the results seen in the combined shear force diagram in Figure 5.10. Here,
the shear forces from LM1 is significantly larger than the ones from LM3. It is therefore
sufficient to only include LM1 in future load combinations and calculations.
Figure 5.9: Bending moment diagram
Figure 5.10: Shear force diagram
Sandwich Arches
55
==PAGE:74==
The conclusion of LM1 being the designing Load Model was expected due to the signifiВӯ
cantly larger forces relative to LM3. Although LM3 has a total of 11 axle loads, and LM1
only has 2, the characteristic axle loads from LM1 of 300kN are more than 15 times larger
than the highest axle loads of LM3, which is 17.8kN. LM1 also includes a UDL, which
increases the related load even further.
5.2.2
ULS Load Combinations
The ULS load combinations and load factors are based on set B+C from Load and CalВӯ
culations Basis for Bridges [22]. Here, the factors depend on the presence of live loads
or not. Factors from equation 6.10a relate to the dead loads being the most unfavourable
load contribution, while factors from 6.10b use live loads as the most unfavourable loads.
Due to the sandwich bridge being in consequence class 3, the factors used for all unВӯ
favourable load contributions are multiplied by a factor, Kfi, of 1.1.
Table 5.2: Load factors ULS

When finding relevant load combinations, it is essential to consider how the forces are
distributed within the structure and which elements contribute to the general capacity. It
is, for example, not enough to check the arch for the highest occurring bending moments
since the arch will experience vast differences related to the normal force. This means that
the most crucial combination could either give the arch the highest compression forces,
tensile forces or bending moments. All cases will have to be computed and analysed
against the capacities of the element.
Table 5.3 helps to explain the 13 different load combinations (LC) and what the combiВӯ
nations are most relevant for when looking at internal forces. Regarding LC1Вӯ5, which
is while the sandwich structure is under construction, is only taken up by the capacity of
the pearlВӯchain arch. Therefore, to find the most significant compression forces, all dead
loads are treated as unfavourable. However, when looking at the most significant bending
moments, all uniformly distributed loads are treated as favourable since they reduce the
bending moment in the arch. LC6Вӯ9, which includes LM1, varies depending on the dead
load being treated as unfavourable or not and the presence of the UDL from LM1. This
is since the dead load would increase the compression in the arch if the arch were to be
checked for any occurring tensile forces. Including UDL from LM1 or not relates to the
possibility of the UDL reducing the negative bending moments of the bridge and will be
analysed further in the report. Lastly, LC10Вӯ13 relates to the splitting forces between the
layers of the sandwich bridge. These splitting forces will be influenced unfavourably by
the live loads and favourably by the dead load of overlaying layers which compress the
layers together.

5.2.4
General distributions of live loads
Load combination 6 to 13 related to the ULS combinations includes live loads from LM1
and are computed to design the most unfavourable load combinations related to differВӯ
ent occurring internal forces in the structure. In addition to the magnitude of the load
combinations, it is also essential to look at the most unfavourable distribution of the load
combinations to the sandwich structure. It is therefore chosen to use four different distriВӯ
butions when applying LC6Вӯ13 to the bridge. The four distributions of LM1 is presented
in Figure 5.11. Here, Distribution 1 is to get the highest overall load at the middle of the
bridge, Distribution 2 is to get the highest load in the middle without the possible favourable
UDL, Distribution 3 is to get the highest load possible in the unfavourable position related
to arches and Distribution 4 is used to get the highest shear forces in the filling.
Figure 5.11: Distributions of LM1 to sandwich bridge

5.3
Internal Forces due to Applied Loads
The internal loads are calculated for the different phases of the erection procedure until the
structure is finished, resulting in a varying response of the structure to the applied loads.
The internal loads are distributed in one way for the arch under erection and in another
way when the sandwich effect is present. This section explains the structure response and
distribution of internal forces in the structure from external loads. The formulas presented
in the section is general representations used to explain the calculation principles. The
full calculations for all Load Combinations and static systems is presented in Appendix D.
5.3.1
Basis for the Calculation of Internal Forces
The different phases of the erection procedure and the finished structure have different
support conditions and various methods for finding related internal forces. Therefore, the
basis for calculating the various internal forces related to the different cases is explained
before the force diagrams are presented.
Static System When Lifting Arch
The loads working on the arch during the lifting procedure is defined by LC1. As a simpliВӯ
fication, the lifting points are designed as vertical reaction forces without horizontal contriВӯ
butions. It is also assumed that the lifting equipment can be tuned so that all three lifting
points lift equal amounts of the arch dead load. Since each lifting load is easy to find by
dividing the dead load of the arch by three, the occurring bending moments, shear forces,
and normal forces can be found by simple direct calculations based on the applied vertical
loads and vertical reaction forces in the lifting points.
Figure 5.12: Placement of reaction forces while lifting arch
The placement of the lifting points is chosen based on the sum of bending moments over
the arch during the lifting procedure. It is preferable to have the arch bend relatively more
inwards than outwards during lifting to have the two ends of the arch slightly closer to
each other when the arch is lifted into its final position. This could potentially eliminate the
need for horizontal jacking of the two ends after the arch is placed in its final position. To
ensure inwards bending rather than outwards, the sum of the bending moment over the
arch during lifting should be negative, which was ensured by the positioning of the lifting
points.
Static System When Arch is Positioned in Place
The static system changes when the arch is lifted into its final position. Then the arch is
supported by one vertical and one horizontal support load in each end, like visualised in
Figure 5.13. This support condition is kept in all arch and sandwich bridge calculations
after the arch is positioned into its final position.
Sandwich Arches
59
==PAGE:78==
Figure 5.13: Reaction forces when arch is positioned in place
The structure above is statically indeterminate and requires slightly more advanced calВӯ
culations to find the horizontal reaction forces in the supports. It is therefore chosen to
use the unitВӯload method to find these forces for every load combination. The unitВӯload
method is based on the five steps:
1. Removing one support condition to make the structure statically determinate.
2. Find a function for the bending moment dependent on the span due to external
forces for the statically determinate system.
3. Apply a unitВӯload to replace the support condition removed initially.
4. Find a function for the bending moment dependent on the span due to the unit load.
This bending moment function relates to the vertical distance of the load to the neuВӯ
tral axis of the crossВӯsection. The neutral axis, and thereby the value of the vertical
distance, changes from the arch neutral axis related to dead loads to the sandwich
structure neutral axis related to live loads. The calculation of this distance is exВӯ
plained further in subsection 5.3.2 and subsection 5.3.3.
5. Find the value of the removed support condition by taking the definite integral over
the span of the two bending moment equations multiplied by each other, divided
by the definite integral over the span of the unit load bending moment equation
squared.
For the arch and sandwich structure calculations specifically, the horizontal reaction force
was found by Equation 5.14, which represents step five from the list above.
RH =
R L

Once the horizontal reaction force is found using the unitВӯload method and the vertical
reaction forces is found using a standard bending moment equilibrium around one of the
supports, all support reaction forces are computed. Then, the bending moment, shear
force and normal force of the different load cases can be calculated. This is explained
further in the coming subsections.
5.3.2
PearlВӯchain Arch Response to Dead Load
The contributions from dead load regarding all load combinations presented in Table 5.4
will be carried by the pearlВӯchain arch solely. The strength of the arch is then provided by
the combination of bending moment, shear force and normal force capacities. Therefore,
it is computed calculations to find occurring bending moments, shear forces, and normal
60
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:79==
forces in the pearlВӯchain arch due to the applied dead loads to be used in later evaluaВӯ
tions against related capacities. All internal loads are calculated dependent on each load
combination, represented by j.
Figure 5.14 is used as a reference figure for the coming functions used to calculate bendВӯ
ing moment, shear force and normal force.
Figure 5.14: External and internal forces in the arch from dead load
Bending Moment in the Arch
When finding the bending moment of the arch, all present dead loads and reaction forces
are multiplied by their respective perpendicular distances to the point being evaluated.
The general formula for the bending moment of the arch is presented in Equation 5.15. In
this equation, the first part multiplies the vertical reaction force by the horizontal distance,
x, from the force to the point being analysed. The second part finds the location of the
dead load resultant and multiplies it with the corresponding value of the resultant. The
last part multiplies the horizontal reaction force by the vertical distance, yc (x), from the
support to the evaluated point.
MEd.A.j (x) = RV A.j В· x вҲ’

gEd.j (x) dx вҲ’RH.j В· yc (x) (5.15)
The calculation used to find the location of the dead load resultant is based on the general
understanding of what taking an integral of a function means. First of all, the integral of
a function calculates the area between the yc (x)=0Вӯline and the function graph. When
this area is calculated, the integral divide the area up into several small columns and
summarises all the columns to find the area. If each column area is multiplied by their
respective distance from the zeroВӯaxis, added together and divided by the total area, the
centre of gravity is found. An example figure of a scaled version of this integration process
is presented in Figure 5.15. This is used to find the location of the resultant force of the
dead load dependent on x. Since the distance is defined as the distance from support A
to the resultant, the final distance is x вҲ’the distance of the resultant.

resultant
The final bending moment of the arch is found related to the dead load contributions from
various load combinations and is presented in Figure 5.16. It is seen from the figure that
the highest positive bending moments in the arch comes from LC3, which represents the
worst distribution case of the filling during the filling procedure. This makes sense due
to LM3 having the highest skew loading of the load cases, with the arch dead load being
treated as favourable. The most significant negative bending moments in the arch comes
from the filling in LC3 and the total dead load of the bridge in LC4 and LC5. The high
negative bending moment from LC3 was assumed early on, but it was more surprising
to see that the total dead loads represented by LC4 and LC5 gave such high negative
bending moments. If the arch calculations were to be simplified by introducing a hinge in
the crown of the arch, which is often the case, the bending moment at this point would
have been zero.

Shear Force in the Arch
Like the bending moment, the shear force is also computed as a function dependent on
x. The shear force is found as the internal force acting perpendicular to the arch curve at
any point of the span. It summarises the vertical external forces and the horizontal forces
separately and decomposes them to fit the direction of the shear force. The decomposing
of forces to the shear force direction is done according to Figure 5.17 which uses the
inclination of the arch curvature at any point of the span and decomposes the forces
using sinus and coВӯsinus of the inclination.
Figure 5.17: Decomposing forces to act perpendicular to the arch curve
The function used to find the shear force is presented in Equation 5.16. The first part takes
into account the horizontal force contribution to the shear force. The last part considers
the vertical contribution as the difference between the vertical reaction force and dead
load contribution to the analysed structure.

Normal Force in Arch
The normal force in the arch is calculated based on the same principles as for the shear
force calculation. The vertical and horizontal forces are decomposed to work parallel to
the arch inclination dependent on x. Figure 5.19 presents the theory used to decompose
the vertical and horizontal forces to the normal force. The arrow representing the normal
force is positioned to work away from the crossВӯsection, making positive values represent
tension and negative represent compression.
Figure 5.19: Decomposing forces to act parallel to the arch curve
Equation 5.17 calculates the normal force in the arch. The two first parts of the equation
decompose the horizontal and vertical forces to work parallel to the arch. The last part
includes the postВӯtensioning force in the pearlВӯchain arch. This is to easily analyse the
normal force diagram to ensure that the arch never experiences any tension forces.

5.3.3
Sandwich Bridge Response to Live Loads
The complete response of the structure changes once the sandwich bridge is fully erected
and the concrete is cured. Now, the bridgeвҖҷs strength comes from a combination of all
components of the bridge and not only the pearlВӯchain arch. The bending moment is taken
as couple forces in the top plate and pearlВӯchain arch, with the filling providing the vertical
distance between the forces. Therefore, the bending moment is initially calculated from
external forces, followed by the distribution of forces from the bending moment to the top
plate and arch. The shear capacity is designed to solely be provided by the shear stress
capacity of the pervious concrete filling and the filling crossВӯsectional area dependent on
x.
Figure 5.20: External and internal forces in the sandwich bridge from live load
Bending Moment
Now that the arch takes the bending moment from dead loads, the remaining bending
moment from live loads must be taken by the full sandwich bridge. The only part that
is different related to the calculation procedure itself regarding the bending moment for
the sandwich structure compared to the arch is the arm of the horizontal reaction force
to the centre axis of the structure. Although the horizontal reaction force is positioned in
the exact location for both instances, the neutral axis changes from the neutral axis of the
arch to the neutral axis of the whole sandwich structure.
Sandwich Arches
65
==PAGE:84==
The vertical distance of the horizontal force from the neutral axis of the arch is defined by
the height of the arch dependent on x, yc (x). The calculation of the vertical distance of
the horizontal reaction force from the neutral axis of the sandwich structure is more comВӯ
plicated. First, the neutral axis of the crossВӯsection dependent on x, zib.SA (x), needs to
be calculated. This equation is found by computing three initial calculations, respectively:
вҖў The neutral axis of the sandwich bridge structure using the SLВӯdeck geometry reВӯ
lated to the middle of each deck.
вҖў The neutral axis of the sandwich bridge structure using the SLВӯdeck geometry reВӯ
lated to the end of each deck.
вҖў The neutral axis of the sandwich bridge structure using the geometry related to the
mortar joint between each SLВӯdeck.
The three neutral axis functions is presented as graphs in Figure 5.21. It is seen from the
figure that the neutral axis of the mortar joint and end geometry of the SLВӯdeck is close to
identical, while the neutral axis using the middle section of the SLВӯdeck is slightly higher
up. This is due to the large proportions of LAC with relatively low youngвҖҷs modulus in the
middle section. As a simplification, the neutral axis function of the structure is defined as
the middle line between the function related to the middle and end SLВӯdeck geometry.
Figure 5.21: The neutral axis functions of the sandwich bridge dependent on the internal
geometry of the SLВӯdeck
When the height of the neutral axis is found dependent on x, the internal bending moment
from live loads can be calculated. The bending moment equation for the sandwich bridge
is similar to the one for the pearlВӯchain arch given as Equation 5.15. The main difference is
the vertical distance of the horizontal load to the neutral axis, zib.SA (x). The bending moВӯ
ment from live loads could also include axle loads as point loads. Equation 5.18 gives the
general bending moment equation for the sandwich structure. a (x) defines the horizontal
distance from the axle load to the point being evaluated, like presented in Figure 5.20.

Normal Force in Top Plate
The normal force in the top plate is calculated based on the bending moment and the
concept of the top plate and arch taking up half of the bending moment each. Therefore,
the normal force of the arch is found by taking half of the bending moment and dividing
it by the vertical distance from the neutral axis of the sandwich structure to the centre of
the top plate, ai.TP (x). Lastly, the postВӯtensioning force in the top plate is included as
a compression force. The calculation is presented by Equation 5.19 together with the
visualisation of the variables used in the calculation in Figure 5.22.
Figure 5.22: Calculation components used to find the normal force in the top plate from
bending moment

The normal force diagrams from all relevant load cases is shown in Figure 5.23. The
highest compression forces in the top plate come from LC6 and LC7 applied at midВӯspan.
LC6 and LC7 include both axle loads and UDL from LM1. The load combinations give
the highest positive bending moments in the sandwich structure and thereby the highest
compression forces in the top plate. The smallest compression forces in the top plate
come from LC6 and LC7 applied at the quarterВӯpoint. These load cases lead to negative
bending moments on the opposite side of the bridge and, therefore, tensile contributions
to the top plate. These tensile forces are used to design the postВӯtensioning reinforcement
required to ensure the top plate is always in compression.

Splitting Force
Splitting between the structural layers of the sandwich structure is documented in the theВӯ
sis вҖқDevelopment and test of arch bridge with materials of different stiffnessвҖқ [19]. ThereВӯ
fore, it is developed some hypothesis regarding this mechanism to try and find a way of
calculating the splitting forces analytically.
Hypothesis 1 is based on the normal force in the PCВӯArch solely. Since the bending
moment capacity is defined by the horizontal couple forces in the top plate and arch, the
idea was that the horizontal force in the arch needed to be decomposed to work in the
plane of the arch. This resulted in the need for a vertical component, which was looked
at as the splitting force. This method is visualised in Figure 5.30.
Figure 5.29: Finding the vertical component of the arch to match the direction of the arch
curvature
The problem with hypothesis 1 was that the splitting force between the top plate and filling
became zero, although it was between these layers that the splitting first occurred during
the scaled tests. Therefore, it was developed a new hypothesis where the neutral axis of
the sandwich structure was used as a reference. The idea was that the bending moment
capacity was taken up by couple forces parallel to the neutral axis graph. This meant
that the normal force in the top plate and arch needed to be decomposed to work in the
Sandwich Arches
71
==PAGE:90==
plane of the neutral axis function. It also resulted in a vertical component for the top plate.
Hypothesis 2 is presented in Figure 5.30.
Figure 5.30: Finding the perpendicular component to the normal forces in the top plate
and arch to match the direction of the sandwich structure neutral axis
The splitting forces related to hypothesis 2 was calculated further dependent on the variВӯ
ous load combinations applied to the Sandwich Arch Bridge. The two equations used to
find the splitting between the upper layer and lower layer is presented by Equation 5.22
and Equation 5.23.

tensile forces are positive and compressive forces negative. It is seen from the diagram
that the most significant splitting forces is found towards the ends of the sandwich bridge.
It is also interesting to see that the highest values come from LC10 applied at midВӯspan
and not LC11 at the quarterВӯpoint.

Figure 5.32 presents the splitting force diagram related to the arch and filling layer. The
diagram shows compressive forces where tensile forces were expected and the other way
around. This indicates that hypothesis 2 is not valid and should not be used further.
Figure 5.32: Splitting force diagram from hypothesis 2 related to the arch and filling layer
The last hypothesis is based on the two previous hypothesises. Here, like in hypothesis
2, the couple forces in the top plate and arch from the bending moment are decomposed
to work in the plane of the sandwich structure neutral axis. However, instead of using the
normal force in the arch, the horizontal component from the bending moment in the arch
is used, similar to hypothesis 1. This results in the splitting force between arch and filling
working opposite to the splitting force from hypothesis 2.

np.PC. Calculations relating to dimensioning preВӯ and postВӯtensioning fores and losses is

6.2
ULS Capacities
Ultimate Limit State (ULS) capacities relates to the calculated ultimate strengths of the
construction parts before collapse or other similar forms of structural failure [28]. Since
the structure gets its bearing capacities from a combined interaction between all structural
elements, the capacity calculations are divided up into individual calculations for each of
the elements. The PCВӯArch is subject to bending moments, normal forces and shear
forces from a combination of live and dead loads. The filling layer needs to withstand
shear forces from live loads and the top plate needs to take up normal forces from live
loads. This section presents the general formulas used in the ULS capacity calculations.
The full calculation procedure is presented in detail in Appendix E. All combined load and
capacity diagrams can be found in the end of Appendix D.
Sandwich Arches
79
==PAGE:98==
6.2.1
Combined Normal Force and Bending Moment Capacity of PCВӯArch
Since the PCВӯArch alone needs to withstand all the dead loads of the Sandwich Arch
Bridge, it will have to take up both significant bending forces, shear forces and normal
forces. Furthermore, the arch contributes with its normal force capacities to take up live
loads when the sandwich effect is present. It is therefore computed calculations to find the
combined bending moment and normal force capacities of the arch in terms of NВӯM interВӯ
action diagrams. The shear capacity of the PCВӯArch is found related to the crossВӯsectional
properties of the SLВӯdeck using standard shear capacity calculations from Eurocode 2,
part 1Вӯ1 [24].
Calculating NВӯM Interaction Diagrams
The combined normal force and bending moment capacity of the PCВӯArch varies over
the span of the bridge due to the changing crossВӯsectional geometries of the SLВӯdeck eleВӯ
ments and mortar joints respectively. Furthermore, both the positive and negative bending
moment capacity should be computed related to the SLВӯdeck geometry. This leads to the
following capacity calculations related to the NВӯM interaction diagrams:
вҖў Positive bending moment capacity Вӯ Middle of SLВӯdeck
вҖў Negative bending moment capacity Вӯ Middle of SLВӯdeck
вҖў Positive bending moment capacity Вӯ End of SLВӯdeck
вҖў Negative bending moment capacity Вӯ End of SLВӯdeck
вҖў Bending moment capacity Вӯ Mortar joint
When computing a capacity diagram related to the combined effect of normal force and
bending force (NВӯM Interaction Diagram), it is relevant to look at some of the key mechaВӯ
nisms that define the outer boundary of the capacity. This outer boundary can be found by
taking inspiration from Figure 6.1 in EC2, part 1Вӯ1 [24], which is presented in Figure 6.2.
This figure presents three general strain distribution that would lead to failure of the crossВӯ
section, which is yielding of reinforcement, pure compression strain limit and crushing of
the concrete. Then the capacity curve is found by calculating the capacities for each of
the three mechanisms and drawing a line between the found values.
Figure 6.2: General strain distribution for reinforced cross section [24]
Calculations related to the positive bending moment capacity of the SLВӯdeck crossВӯsection
80
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:99==
in the PC arch is used as a reference when explaining the following procedure. When
computing the NВӯM interaction diagram for this specific crossВӯsection, 6 different failing
mechanisms have been analysed and calculated respectively. The 6 mechanisms are
presented in Figure 6.3 and referred to as i in the general formulas. The reason behind
choosing six mechanisms to analyse and not only three is to get a more detailed capacity
curve that better visualise the response of the complicated SLВӯdeck crossВӯsection. The
calculations regarding negative bending moment capacity of the SLВӯdeck cross section
and the calculations for the mortar joint is shown in Appendix E.
Figure 6.3: Possible strain distributions for positive bending moment in the ultimate limit
state
The capacities during the various mechanisms presented in Figure 6.3 are calculated
looking at the perpendicular force contributions during the different strain distributions.
The final compression capacity, NRd.SL.pos.i, is given by Equation 6.8 dependent on the
different failing mechanisms, i. This capacity is found by calculating the difference beВӯ
tween the initial compression capacity of the concrete section in compression, NC, preВӯ
sented in Equation 6.10, and the compression forces from preВӯ and postВӯtensioning reВӯ
inforcement, Np.(top/mid/bot) and N(p.p.d/p.p), given by Equation 6.13 and Equation 6.15.
The bending moment capacity for each failing mechanism, MRd.SL.pos.i, is found by usВӯ
ing Equation 6.9 which multiplies the various internal forces with the relative distances,
yC/p.top/p.mid/p.bot/p.p, from the neutral axis of the crossВӯsection. It is important to notice
that it is used two different force contributions from the postВӯtensioning related to compresВӯ
sive capacity and bending moment capacity. The force contribution from postВӯtensioning
used in the normal force capacity calculation, Np.p.d, is only the change in postВӯtensioning
due to the change in strain, Оөp.p.d, from each failing mechanism. This is since the postВӯ
tensioning force is added to the normal force contributions from external loads calculated
in subsubsection 5.3.3 to more easily control that the PC arch is always in compression.
The bending moment contribution from the full postВӯtensioning force, Np.p, is included in
the capacity calculations since this bending moment addition is not added in any other
section.
NRd.SL.pos.i = NC вҲ’Np.top вҲ’Np.mid вҲ’Np.bot вҲ’Np.p.d
(6.8)
MRd.SL.pos.i = NC В· yC вҲ’Np.top В· yp.top вҲ’Np.mid В· yp.mid вҲ’Np.bot В· yp.bot вҲ’Np.p В· yp.p (6.9)
Sandwich Arches
81
==PAGE:100==
Figure 6.4: Internal forces and distances to SLВӯdeck neutral axis
The initial concrete compression capacity, NC, given by Equation 6.10 is dependent in
the area of the concrete section in compression and the design value of the concrete
compressive strength, fcd. Since the strain distribution from Figure 6.3 show compression
as part of the right side of the vertical line, it is easy to see that the magnitude of the
compression zone for each failing mechanism decreases from failing mechanism 1 to 6.
The height of this zone, Оұd, is calculated by finding an equation representing the slope
of the strain line based on the two known strains for each mechanism, and then solving
for the distance from the top to where the strain is zero. Since the compression stress
distribution related to this ОұdВӯzone is not square, but rather decreases slightly towards
the zero strain line, it needs to be multiplied by som factors stated in defined in EC2,
part 1Вӯ1, section 3.1.7 [24]. The first factor takes into account the effective height of the
compressive zone, О». The zone is also multiplied by a factor, О·, which is taking into account
the compressive strength of the concrete. When the zone is multiplied by the relevant
factors, it can be calculated as a square zone. Since the amount of strong concrete
decreases towards the bottom of the SLВӯdeck it makes it more complicated to calculate
the area of strong concrete when finding the negative bending moment capacity since the
compressive zone is then located at the bottom. Therefore, to have a general function
of the concrete compressive capacity, it is chosen to use bC.SL (h), which is given as
Equation 3.1 in subsection 3.5.2. This equation gives the width of the concrete at any point
in the SLВӯdeck dependent on the crossВӯsectional distance from the bottom of the SLВӯdeck
to the relative point. The area of concrete for each failing mechanism can then easily
be found by taking the definite integral of bC.SL (h) over the height of the compressive
zone, which is done in Equation 6.10. This area is then multiplied by fcd to find the initial
compressive capacity of the concrete.

The force distances used to find the bending moment capacity in Equation 6.9 are all

This is visualised in Figure 6.4. Since the height of the compression zone varies depenВӯ

varying. Equation 6.18 is made as a general formula to calculate the arm of the compresВӯ
sive force to the neutral axis. The numerator of the first part of this equation takes the
definite integral of the concrete width function over the height of the compression zone
which in practise is dividing the function up into thousands of small pieces, finding the

area of the pieces and adding them together. If this definite integral is also multiplied by
the height of each individual point, h, then the definite integral summarises all these small
areas and the relative h multiplied by every single area. Then this value is divided by the
total area of the concrete compressive zone to find the distance of the compressive resulВӯ
tant from the bottom of the SLВӯdeck. Lastly, the height of the neutral axis from the bottom
is subtracted to find the distance of the compressive zone resultant from the neutral axis.

The NВӯM interaction diagram for the SLВӯdeck crossВӯsection related to positive bending
moments can be seen in Figure 6.5 and the negative bending moment in Figure 6.6. It
can be seen from the two diagrams that the blue line, representing the middle section of
the SLВӯdeck, has the lowest capacity related to the positive bending moment, but the red
line, representing the end section, has the lowest capacity related to the negative bending
moment. This is mainly related to the postВӯtensioning cable and its curved geometry which
results in the cable being closer to the neutral axis in the middle section related to the end
section. The postВӯtensioning cable actually reduces the bending moment capacity related
to negative bending moment since it is positioned underneath the neutral axis of the cross
section and thereby reducing the capacity more at the end section where the distance from
the neutral axis is bigger.

The NВӯM interaction diagram regarding the mortar joint between the SLВӯdeck elements is

This diagram is presented in Figure 6.7 together with the diagrams for the other two secВӯ
tions. It is seen from Figure 6.7 that the mortar joint has larger bending moment capacities
related to the SLВӯdeck when subject to higher normal forces. This comes from the mortar
joint having a larger portion of concrete area per crossВӯsection to take up the compressive
forces combined with the use of C80 concrete compared to the C55 concrete used in the
SLВӯdecks. However, since the mortar joint have less reinforcement, the capacity of the
mortar joint is reduced significantly for smaller normal force contributions, which makes it
relevant to include the crossВӯsection in future capacity evaluations.

Evaluating NВӯM Interaction Capacities Against Actual Forces from Load
Combinations
The bending moments in the PCВӯArch from subsubsection 5.3.2 is combined with the
normal forces from subsubsection 5.3.3 and included in the relevant NВӯM Interaction DiВӯ
agrams below. The evaluation of the capacity is based on the principle that all occurring
forces should be on the left side of the capacity line. If the internal forces moves over to
the right side of the capacity line, the pearlВӯchain capacity is exceeded and the geometry
needs to be improved. Figure 6.8 presents the positive bending moments in the arch with
the related normal forces. It is seen from the diagram that the load combinations closest
to the capacity line is LC2 and LC3 which both represent the highest skew loading related
to the filling procedure.
Sandwich Arches
85
==PAGE:104==
Figure 6.8: NВӯM interaction diagram for positive bending moment with actual forces in
arch due to load combinations
Figure 6.9 presents the negative bending moments in the arch with related normal forces.
What is interesting about the data presented in this figure is that the load combinations
closest to the capacity line is not the ones with the highest bending moments. The load
combination closest to the capacity line is rather LC6 which represents the total dead load
and LM1 with all loads treated as unfavourable applied at midВӯspan. This load combinaВӯ
tion applies high bending moments to the arch from live load which is giving high tensile
contributions to the arch at the middle of the span and thereby reducing the normal force
significantly. All dead loads is also treated as unfavourable resulting in high negative
bending moments in the arch itself.

arch due to load combinations

6.2.2
Shear Capacity of Pervious Concrete Filling Layer
The shear capacity of the filling layers is based on the shear stress capacities found by
testing and stated in the article вҖқPervious concrete fill in PearlВӯChain Bridges: Using smallВӯ
scale results in fullВӯscale implementationвҖқ [25]. Here, the characteristic shear stress caВӯ
pacity of the pervious concrete filling, fpcvk, is found to be 1.3 MPa. The design capacity,
fpcvd is then calculated by dividing the characteristic capacity by the concrete material
factor, Оіc = 1.4. The shear force capacity is then found by multiplying the shear stress
capacity by the crossВӯsectional area of the filling layer dependent on x, which is presented
by
VRd.c.f (x) = fpcvd В· bSL.a В· (f вҲ’y (x) + hf.top)
(6.27)
Figure 6.12 presents the shear force capacity lines of the pervious concrete filling layer
and related shear forces occurring in the filling layer from live loads. The figure shows that
the designing shear forces is found at midВӯspan from load combinations applied at midВӯ
span. This is since the crossВӯsectional height of the filling layer increases significantly
towards the supports of the bridge. The filling height above the crown can easily be
increased or decreased based on the shear force at the middle, which is done for the
presented case to optimise material use.

6.2.3
Normal Force Capacity of Top Plate
The capacity of the top plate is defined by the compression capacity of the concrete and
tensioning force provided by the postВӯtensioning reinforcement. The postВӯtensioning is
defined by the required force to keep the top plate from experiencing any tension forces.
Once the required postВӯtensioning force is obtained and applied to the load configuration
as compression, the capacity can be defined by the compressive stress capacity multiplied
by the crossВӯsectional area of the concrete. This is presented in Equation 6.28.
NRd.TP = вҲ’fcd.TP В· AC.TP
(6.28)
The capacity of the top plate is presented as the capacity line in Figure 6.13 together with
the actual forces occurring in the top plate.
Figure 6.13: Normal force diagrams with normal force capacity line related to the top plate
90
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:109==
The capacity and related material of the top plate is optimised based on both concrete
quality and crossВӯsectional height. The height of the top plate should be big enough to fit
the required cover thickness stated in subsection 3.5.3 as cp.min = 60mm. This means
that the height should fit at least two times the minimum cover thickness and the diameter
of the postВӯtensioning reinforcement. When using for example 7 cables, with the diameter
65 mm [42], like the example used in the 27 m sandwich bridge, the top plate needs to be
at least 185 mm thick. This resulted in the use of concrete quality C35 rather than better
concrete qualities with greater GWP to reduce the environmental impact.
6.3
SLS Capacities
SLS capacity calculations are done focusing on stress limitation in relevant sections for
all relevant loads affecting in the Sandwich Arch Bridge erection and lifetime. Calculation
of stress is done for compression and tensile stress in SL section mid and end, in the top
and bottom section fibre, as well as the concrete section in top and bottom section fibre
and compression in the mortar joint. Illustration of the sections can be seen in Figure 3.9.
SLS capacities are calculated for all Sandwich Arch Bridge designs listed in Table 4.1
and Table 4.2 and are continuing addressed by relevant design number. All relevant SLS
calculations can be found in Appendix F.
6.3.1
PostВӯtensioning
Calculations of the concrete stress from postВӯtensioning, as illustrated in Figure 4.3, are
done similar to procedure explained in subsection 3.5.3. All forces acting on the cross
section from preВӯ and postВӯtensioning is divided on the ideal section area and moment
contribution from preВӯand postВӯtensioning are included by multiplying the force with the
distance to section natural axis, divided on the second moment of area. Differences in
distances to natural axis is causing different moment contributions for each section. The
postВӯtensioning after immediate loss, Pim.PC(x), is used in the calculations. Both the ideal
section area, Ai, and the second moment of area, Wit and Wib, varies for SL, concrete
and joint section. The joint section is only affected by compressive stress from the postВӯ
tensioning cable. Generalised equation for the concrete stress in top and bottom is given
in Equation 6.29 and Equation 6.30. The resulting concrete stress from postВӯtensioning
of PCВӯArch design nr. 1 is plotted in Figure 6.14.

From the plot it is clear that the concrete stress from postВӯtensioning is within limits, with
compressive stress in all sections at both top and bottom. The extremes are found as
max compressive stress in the SL bottom end section, similar to what was found in subВӯ
section 3.5.3, and min compressive stress in the top of the concrete section. Looking into
results from the other cases listed in Table 4.1 and Table 4.2, all concrete stress values
are within limits. Differences in the extreme value for compression stress in the SL botВӯ
tom end section is seen to decrease with increased span as assumed due to increased
section height. For increased SLВӯdeck width of 2400mm the concrete stress values was
similar.
6.3.2
PCВӯArch lifting
For the calculation of the concrete stress from lifting of the PCВӯArch, as illustrated in FigВӯ
ure 4.4, the results from postВӯtensioning stress is still affecting the section. Further, the
bending moment, Mg.l(x), and normal force, Ng.l(x), occurring in the arch from lifting at
three positions is included in all sections in both top and bottom fibre. Calculations of the
bending moment and normal force is done using characteristic values, no loading factors.
Generalised equation for the concrete stress in top and bottom is given in Equation 6.31
and Equation 6.32 with factors varying for each section. The resulting concrete stress
from lifting of PCВӯArch design nr. 1 is plotted in Figure 6.15.

From the plot it is clear that the concrete stress from lifting of the PCВӯArch is within limits,
with compressive stress in all sections at both top and bottom. The extreme values are
found at the lifting points, with max compressive stress in in the SL bottom end section,
and min compressive stress at the mortar joint section. Similar response is found for
increased span and increased section width. For cases with 50m span, tensile stress
occurs in the top fibre in both SL top end section and joint section, but are still within
limits. Increasing number of lifting point should be considered for increased span above
50m.
6.3.3

The load from sequential filling of pervious concrete on the arch, as illustrated in FigВӯ

stress is still affecting the cross section, but additional bending moment, Mg.Af(x), and
normal force, Ng.Af(x), from the fill material dead load is included. Calculations of the
bending moment and normal force is done using characteristic values, no loading factors.
Generalised equation for the concrete stress in top and bottom is given in Equation 6.33
and Equation 6.34 with factors varying for each section. The resulting concrete stress
from sequential filling on the PCВӯArch design nr. 1 is plotted in Figure 6.16.

at the crown, with max concrete compression in the SL bottom end section, and on the
left side of the arch with full fill height causing minimal compression in the bottom fibre
and increased compression in the top fibre. Similar response is found for increased span
and section width, but tensile forces in the bottom fibre is evident for both increased span
and section width, but within limits. For increased span the concrete volume increases
resulting in bigger loads. For increased section widths, the load acting on each arch
increases, further increasing the stresses.
6.3.4
Total deadВӯload
The total dead load acting on the PCВӯArch includes the pervious concrete fill material
on the sides and above crown and the concrete top plate. The postВӯtensioning stress
is still affecting the cross section, but additional bending moment, Mg.tot(x), and normal
force, Ng.tot(x), from fill material and top plate dead load is included. Calculations of the
bending moment and normal force is done using characteristic values, no loading factors.
Generalised equation for the concrete stress in top and bottom is given in Equation 6.35
and Equation 6.36 with factors varying for each section. The resulting concrete stress
from total dead load on the PCВӯArch for design nr. 1 is plotted in Figure 6.17.
Пғc.top.gtot(x) = Пғc.top.p(x) + Ng.tot(x)
Ai
вҲ’Mg.tot(x)
Wit
(6.35)
Пғc.bot.gtot(x) = Пғc.top.p(x) + Ng.tot(x)
Ai
+ Mg.tot(x)
Wib
(6.36)
94
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:113==
Figure 6.17: Concrete stress from total deadВӯload
From the plot it is clear that the concrete stress from total dead load is within limits, with
compressive stress in all sections at both top and bottom fibre. The concrete stress is
close to the limit, but with future loads acting on the sandwich structure taken as mainly
tensile force in the PCВӯArch, increased compression it is seen as favourable. Extreme
values are found at the crown, with max concrete compression in the bottom of the SL
end section, and on each side of the arch with minimal compression in the bottom fibre.
Additional evenly distributed loads from fill above crown and top plate is seen to normalise
the stress distribution caused by fill on the sides of the PCВӯArch. Similar response is found
for increased span and section width, but decreased concrete compression in SL bottom
end and mid section is evident caused by the increased load.
The weight of the fill material is seen to have great implications on the concrete stress
from both sequential filling and total dead load. Reducing the weight of the fill material
in the sides could have future potential in regards to optimisation of the Sandwich Arch
Bridge concept.
6.3.5
Characteristic load combination
All loads applied after curing of fill material and top plate is distributed to the PCВӯArch and
top plate based on the sandwich concept as described in section 4.1. The concrete stress
from total dead load will constantly affect the PCВӯArch in further combinations. Additional
normal force in the arch from live loads, Nq.PC(x), will effect the final concrete stress.
Generalised equation for the concrete stress in top and bottom is given in Equation 6.37
and Equation 6.38 with factors varying for each section. The resulting concrete stress
from characteristic load combination for design nr. 1 is plotted in Figure 6.18.
Пғc.top.q(x) = Пғc.top.gtot(x) + Nq.A(x)
Ai
(6.37)
Пғc.bot.q(x) = Пғc.bot.gtot(x) + Nq.A(x)
Ai
(6.38)
Sandwich Arches
95
==PAGE:114==
Figure 6.18: Concrete stress from characteristic load combination: LM1 + DeadВӯload
From the plot it is clear that the concrete stress from characteristic load combination is
within limits, with compressive stress in all sections at both top and bottom fibre. Extreme
values are similar to what was found for total dead load, but the live load positioned on left
side of the arch is moving the position of the max concrete compression in SL bottom end
section to the right of the crown. The response of the sandwich structure, affecting the
arch with mainly tensile stress will cause high capacity when the arch is effected by mainly
compressive stress before additional live loads. Similar response is found for increased
span and section width, all within concrete stress limits.
All calculations have been using the dimentioning postВӯtensioning force after immediate
loss, Pim.PC(x), however the postВӯtensioning force after long term load is also relevant for
calculation of the concrete stresses from total dead load and live load. Looking at the conВӯ
crete stress plot for these situations, the concrete compression is seen to be dimensional,
and tensile stress is well below limits. Long term loss from tendon relaxation will reduce
the force being applied to the concrete, resulting in reduced compression. The concrete
stress after longВӯterm loss is therefore assumed to be within limits. The concrete stress
in the top plate of the sandwich structure from postВӯtensioning and characteristic loads is
found to be within limits, and are not discussed further.

Chapter 7:
Bridge Design Comparison
Numerous studies have been done on Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) of bridge strucВӯ
tures, which aims to evaluate the environmental impacts of the structure during its whole
life. The study Environmental Life Cycle Assessment of Bridges [43] presented a deВӯ
tailed comparative environmental LCA case study of three build bridges in Norway. The
study concluded that the production of materials for the main loadВӯcarrying systems and
the abutments account for the main share of the environmental impacts.
The study
An OptimisationВӯLCA of a Prestressed Concrete Precast Bridge [44] analysed a costВӯ
optimised prestressed concrete precast bridge using two different approaches. The study
concluded that out of the system boundary activitiesНҫ Manufacturing, Construction, Use
and Maintenance and End of life, the manufacturing phase and the use and maintenance
phase are the ones with the higher environmental impact. A case study done in ChinaНҫ
Estimation of carbon dioxide emission in highway construction [45] proposed an empiriВӯ
cal method, estimating the total emission from different steps of construction processНҫ raw
material production, material transportation and onsite construction. The study concluded
that in general, over 80 percent of the CO2 emission was generated from raw material proВӯ
ductionНҫ the onВӯsite construction and material transportation only accounted for 10 and 3
percent of the whole CO2 emission, respectively.
The studies show that using the CO2 emission from material production will provide a
good measurement of total CO2 emission and will be an effective way to compare bridge
designs. Material production emission is found in a products Environmental Product DecВӯ
laration (EPD). An EPD is a declaration, verified to the requirements in ISO 14025 [46],
including information about the lifeВӯcycle environmental impact of products developed in
accordance with the requirements of EN 15804+A1 [47]. The EPD categorise the prodВӯ
ucts environmental impact in four stagesНҫ Product stage, construction process stage, use
stage and end of life stage as seen in Figure 7.1. The product stage (A1 Вӯ A3), indicated
in Figure 7.1, includes all activities relating to the production and will dictate the Global
Warming Potential (GWP) for the product, in kg CO2 equivalents pr declared unit.

The advantages of the Sandwich Arch Bridge is not only confined to optimising the maВӯ
terial usage, but previous studies, as well as the first PCВӯBridge, Vorgod Creek Road
Bridge, built in Jutland in Denmark, discuss the reduction in construction time using preВӯ
fabricated elements. Reducing the construction time could have multiple benefits for the
overall project concerning CO2 emissionНҫ one could be reducing the duration of traffic
diversion. All relevant calculations for the chapter can be found in Appendix G.
7.1
Bridge information
The Sandwich Arch Bridge design is compared to a typical concrete bridge design used
for highway crossings, a continuously ThreeВӯSpan Bridge. VosnГҰsvej Bridge, bridge 72Вӯ
0033, is a postВӯtensioned, continuously ThreeВӯSpan concrete bridge with a total length of
approx. 55m and an effective width of 9m builtВӯin 1985. The bridge is situated in Г…rhus
municipality, crossing M72 Djurlandsmotorvejen north of LГёgten. Bridge information is
gathered from original bridge drawings, see Appendix H, and a renovation report by COWI
published by the Danish road administration, Vejdirektoratet [48].
The distance between intermediate supports for VosnГҰsvej bridge is 26.729m, spanning
over a 4Вӯlane motorway, similar to design nr. 1 and 2 listed in section 4.3. To investigate
the different bridge designs for a relevant increased span, the basic crossВӯsection of 6Вӯ
lane motorways consisting of six lanes with a total width of 35m, given in Vejdirektoratet
cross profile manual [49]. Finding the necessary bridge dimensions for increased span
will indicate which bridge design is favourable for increased span and help indicate GWP
development relating to bridge span.
7.1.1
ThreeВӯSpan Bridge
The ThreeВӯSpan Bridge is cast as a continuous bridge with a total of four supports, two
on each end and two intermediate supports. When comparing the design against a SandВӯ
wich Arch Bridge, the total length of all three spans has to be included when these are
a part of the ThreeВӯSpan Bridges static system. The ThreeВӯSpan design reduces the
loadВӯresponse of the main span, as illustrated in Figure 7.2. When looking at the main
span only, the bending moment is taken as a positive bending moment only between the
columns (BC), illustrated with the red moment response. Looking at the continuous beam,
the bending moment is taken as both positive and negative bending moment, reducing
the extreme value as illustrates with the blue moment response.

7.1.2
Sandwich Arch Bridge
The Sandwich Arch Bridge design is only spanning over the main span of 27m and 35m.
The resulting design parameters is a result of ULS and SLS calculations, as illustrated
in section 6.2 and section 6.3. SLВӯdeck width used in both designs is 1650mm, with an
additional 100mm for the hammerhead joint. The number of arches in the bridge is 6,
where the end arches are only 1700mm, resulting in an effective width of 10400mm. The
Sandwich Arch Bridge is designed without concrete side walls, contrasting the first SandВӯ
wich Arch Bridge built in Jutland in Denmark. Formwork is assumed to be connected to
the side arches before lifting and removed after casting the pervious concrete fill. Further
on, safety rails are connected on the sides of the top plate with connections through to the
pervious concrete fill. The Sandwich Arch Bridge design can be seen in Figure 7.4, with
relevant parameters listed in Table 7.3 complying with design nr. 1 and 3 in section 4.3.

7.2
Material Production GWP
All material production GWP numbers are collected from producers EPD or the Danish
EPD database [51].
Concrete
Based on the aggressive environmental class, the minimum concrete compressive strength
to be used in both bridge structures is fck = 35 MPa to comply with indicative strength
classes for durability, protecting both the reinforcement and the concrete against damВӯ
age[30]. This is the case for reinforced concrete where water could accumulate, and not
the pervious concrete where the water will be drained and no accumulation is evident
[25]. Concrete C35 is found to have a production GWP, GWPC35, of 362kg CO2/m3. In
100
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:119==
comparison, C45 have a production GWP, GWPC45, of 426kg CO2/m3, given in EPDвҖҷs
from Dansk Beton Вӯ Fabriksbetonforeningen, covering multiple suppliers in Denmark [52]
[53]. Finding GWP for other concrete strength is done by assuming a linear progression
from 0 through the given values for C35 and C45.
GWP(x) = x + aGWP В· x
(7.1)
with aGWP equal to the function rise factor between GWPC35 for x equal to 35 and
GWPC45 for x equal to 45, resulting in aGWP = 8.85. Finding the GWP for C55, GWPC55,
by substitution x = 55 in Equation 7.1 shown in Equation 7.2
GWPC55 = GWP(55) = 55 + 8.85 В· 55 = 542kg/m3
(7.2)
Production GWP for LAC concrete, GWPLAC, is 194kg CO2/m3 given in EPD from Dansk
Beton Вӯ Blokforeningen [54]. EPD for pervious concrete is not published at the Danish EPD
database or any relevant producers. To find the production GWP for pervious concrete,
a method where the cement content is compared to relevant published concrete EPD
are used. First, a lower bound value could be found using the linear progression as
illustrated with Equation 7.1. Using the compressive strength found for pervious concrete,
fpck=10MPa [25] the resulting lower bound GWP, GWPpc.lb, is 98.5kg CO2/m3. Using
the cement density for pervious concrete, ПҒce.pc, of 317.6kg CO2/m3 as implemented in
the first PCВӯbridge [25], the GWP is calculated by multiplying the GWP for C35 with a
factor Оҙc, representing the cement density difference between the pervious concrete and
concrete. The cement content for concrete is assumed to be the average percentage
given in relevant EPD.
Оҙc.C35 = ПҒce.pc
ПҒce.C35
= 317.6kg/m3
396.7kg/m3 = 0.801
(7.3)
GWPpc.C35 = GWPC35 В· Оҙc.C35 = 362kg/m3 В· 0.801 = 290kg/m3
(7.4)
As mentioned earlier, the pervious concrete will not accumulate any water because of the
high void content draining any water. Thus the method comparing the cement density
of the pervious concrete could be done for a concrete type that could be used in lower
environmental classes. Comparing the cement density to that of concrete C25, the lowest
regular concrete EPD at the danish database, with a production GWP of 241kg CO2/m3,
when assuming the average percentage of cement content [55].
Оҙc.C25 = ПҒce.pc
ПҒce.C25
= 317.6kg/m3
309.7kg/m3 = 1.03
(7.5)
GWPpc.C25 = GWPC25 В· Оҙc.C25 = 241kg/m3 В· 1.03 = 247kg/m3
(7.6)
Steel
For both reinforcement and tensioning steel, the same production GWP is used. The
production GWP for steel is 473kg/tonne given in Celsa Steel Service using recycled
steel and implementing environmentally friendly power sources for manufacturing [56].
Resulting GWP for each m3 of steel, GWPsteel, is 3713 kg/m3 when using the standardised
density for steel ПҒs = 7850 kg/m3 [26].

To illustrate the increase in material GWP relating to an increased span, the material
GWP values are plotted for both the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge and Sandwich Arch Bridge. The
104
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:123==
increase is assumed to follow an exponential function found between the obtained material
GWP points for the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge, 27 and 35m, and Sandwich Arch Bridge, 27 and
50m. Figure 7.8 illustrates the resulting GWP for the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge as blue dots with
the exponential function going through these points as a solid blue line. The resulting
GWP for the Sandwich Arch Bridge is given as red dots, with the exponential function
going through GWP for 27m and 50m as a solid red line. The solid black line represents
the exponential function for material GWP for the Sandwich Arch Bridge when using the
upper bound values for pervious concrete. In contrast, the dotted black line illustrates the
function using lower bound values. GWP savings are given as the green line, which is
the difference between the blue and red function lines.
Figure 7.8: Material GWP plot
It is clear from the figure that the material GWP savings for using a Sandwich Arch Bridge
instead of a ThreeВӯSpan Bridge is not evident for a bridge with a main span of less than вҲј
25m. The exponential function is seen to be a relative god representation of the material
GWP values. For a Sandwich Arch Bridge with a 35m span, the function gives a material
GWP value of 183 887 kg CO2 eq while the calculated value was 195 927 kg CO2 eq,
giving a marginal error of вҲј6%. The functions are found between the resulting values
and results for lower spans, under 10m, maybe outputting a higher value than the real.
Implementing the Sandwich Arch Bridge design in increased spans above 35m is seen
to be functional, and GWP savings will increase with an increased span. The material
GWP value for pervious concrete is representing uncertainties and are causing a high
total material GWP for the sandwich structure. If the GWP value for pervious concrete is
reduced, the material GWP savings of the Sandwich Arch Bridge design is increased.
Calculations were done to investigate the differences in transportation GWP (A4 in FigВӯ
ure 7.1) to see the effects in transportation GWP using preВӯcast elements in the Sandwich
Arch Bridge instead of inВӯsitu concrete as used in the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge. The total transВӯ
portation GWP of all material used in the different components in the Sandwich Arch
Bridge design was вҲјtwo times that of the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge but was under 5% of the
GWP value from material production for both bridge design for all spans. The increase
in transportation GWP was for the investigated construction caused by the distance from
the SLВӯdeck production factory to the construction site. Currently, there is only one proВӯ
duction factory for SLВӯdeck in Denmark, but more factories are on the way in the future,
which could reduce the transportation GWP for future projects. Focusing on the material
Sandwich Arches
105
==PAGE:124==
production GWP, a better picture is given on the overall sustainability of the design, where
the transportation GWP will vary depending on the construction siteвҖҷs location.
7.4
Construction Process optimisation
The possible advantages of using the Sandwich Arch Bridge design are not only confined
to the material and CO2 savings, but the usage of preВӯcast elements could optimise the
construction process giving a reduced construction time. An estimated time schedule for
constructing a Sandwich Arch Bridge at VosnГҰsvej is made using the time schedule from
the renovation report by COWI published by Vejdirektoratet [48]. Similar activities are
assumed to have the same duration for both bridge designs. Time schedules for both
ThreeВӯSpan Bridge and Sandwich Arch Bridge can be seen in Table 7.8 and Table 7.9
where the custom activities relating to the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge is listed with red text, and
custom activities for the Sandwich Arch Bridge is listed with green text.
Construction activities relating to increased construction time for the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge is
scaffolding and reinforcement of the bridge deck. During the period when the scaffolding
is established, the motorway going under the bridge is reduced to only one lane in each
direction for safety reasons. The establishment of the course and demolition of the crash
barrier is done so that the lane can be moved horizontally throughout the scaffolding
period.
For the Sandwich Arch Bridge, the foundation formwork and casting is a timeВӯconsuming
activity. The resulting horizontal reaction force at the abutments is causing increased
foundation size resulting in a higher estimated duration. The construction of the PCВӯ
Arches onВӯsite includes formwork and casting of joints, postВӯtensioning and grouting of the
cable duct. Before lifting the PCВӯArches, the formwork can be added to the PCВӯArches on
the sides so that fill material can be poured after reinforcement and casting of the adjacent
joint is done.

An estimated total construction time savings when using Sandwich Arch Bridge design is
calculated from the total construction time in Table 7.8 and Table 7.9 to 91 days, resulting
106
Sandwich Arches
==PAGE:125==
in a percentage reduction of вҲј45%. The reduction in construction time can be assumed
to increase with increased span when the scaffolding and reinforcement of the bridge deck
for the ThreeВӯSpan Bridge design increases. The reduction of total construction time could
have substantial effects on the cost of the project.
When using the Sandwich Arch Bridge design, the traffic disturbance will also be reduced.
The total traffic diversion time over the bridge will be reduced by the reduction in total
construction time. Further, when establishing the scaffolding for the bridge deck for the
ThreeВӯSpan Bridge design, the highway traffic is affected by the reduced number of driving
files, causing higher traffic and possible queuing in rush hours. Lifting the PCВӯArches into
position above the highway will close the highway for traffic in an estimated period of 1
day. Further construction on the Sandwich Arch Bridge can be done while full traffic under
the bridge on the highway is permitted.

==PAGE:127==
8
Conclusion
This thesis aims to investigate identified and addressed structural challenges regarding
the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept analytically. Based on the findings throughout this
thesis, the hypothesis is proven to be correct, and the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept is
competitive.
First, the relevant theory regarding Arch Bridges, PearlВӯChain technology, SuperВӯLight
concrete deck used in PearlВӯChain Arches and the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept was
studied. An analytical investigation was conducted to find the optimal arch configuration
in the Sandwich Arch Bridge structure. Based on investigations of hinge configuration,
arch centre line shape and riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio, the optimal configuration was a circular two
hinged arch with a riseВӯtoВӯspan ratio between 1/10 Вӯ 1/14. Increased fill height above arch
crown was found to reduce the bending moment load on the arch but will also increase
the horizontal reaction force, total weight, cost, environmental impact and installation time
of the Sandwich Arch Bridge.
Investigation into previously found cracks from postВӯtensioning of the PCВӯArch was conВӯ
ducted. Analytical representation of SLВӯdeck sectional properties was calculated and used
to find occurring concrete stress from postВӯtensioning. Concrete compression in the botВӯ
tom of the SLВӯdeck, close to the end, was critical. Further, increased postВӯtensioning caВӯ
ble rise within the SLВӯdeck element was favourable. A visual representation of the cable
rise and limits for arch span and SLВӯdeck configurations were presented. Maximum postВӯ
tensioning cable dimensions were presented for several predetermined SLВӯdeck sectional
configuration to be within cracking limits.
To find a correlation between the PCВӯArch total weight and mobile crane lifting capacities,
the calculation of the total weight of PCВӯArches with different SLВӯdeck configurations was
computed. A total PCВӯArch weight below mobile crane lifting capacity can reduce cost and
construction time. Lifting capacities for multiple mobile cranes were found and presented
visually, dependent on PCВӯArch weights for different arch spans. The visualisation could
then be used in preliminary design to find the SLВӯdeck configuration to be implemented in
the PCВӯArch.
Relevant dead loads and live loads were combined into load combinations and computed
as functions dependent on the position along the horizontal axis. Internal bending moВӯ
ment, shear and normal forces from the load combination were computed analytically as
functions, which provided more exact results and fewer manual calculations. The analytВӯ
ical calculations were made so that results would automatically change if the parameters
of the bridge were altered.
To investigate the causes of the previously seen splitting occurring between layers, three
different examples of possible calculation methods to represent the force were explained.
All examples are based on finding vertical components between the layers based on the
internal bending moment in the Sandwich Arch Bridge from external live loads. Each
explanation showed potential for representing the splitting force. It could also be a comВӯ
bination of multiple explanations. The splitting force calculations require more extensive
investigations in order to validate the outcome.
Capacities of the Sandwich Arch Bridge elements were found analytically as functions
which made it possible to present as graphs. To validate each bridge element, capacities
Sandwich Arches
109
==PAGE:128==
and relevant internal forces were presented as graphs and compared at any position along
the horizontal axis. The arch capacity was defined by the shear capacity and combined NВӯ
M interaction capacity of the SLВӯdeck and mortar joints. The filling capacity was defined
solely by the shear stress capacity of the pervious concrete filling. The top plate was
defined by the compressive capacity of the concrete crossВӯsection and the required force
applied by the postВӯtensioning.
The most critical load combinations regarding the PCВӯArch before the sandwich concept
was evident were the filling procedure and the total dead load. The load representing the
filling sequence was defined by filling up to the crown on one side of the arch and one
less layer of filling on the other side. The filling sequence gave high bending moments
and shear forces in the arch, which resulted in critical loads for both SLS and ULS capacВӯ
ities. The total dead load gave the most critical compressive forces in the arch related to
compression cracking in SLS. When the sandwich concept was evident, the critical load
combinations were the total dead load combined with LM1 applied at midВӯspan. This load
case reduced the compression force in the arch due to the significant bending moment in
the sandwich structure from LM1 combined with the small vertical distance between the
arch and the neutral axis. The reduction of the normal force resulted in a more critical
case related to the NВӯM interaction in the arch.
The most critical load case regarding the shear force in the filling and compressive force
in the top plate was LM1 applied at midВӯspan. This load distribution gave the highest
shear forces in the filling at the middle of the bridge, where the filling layer is thinnest.
The same load case was critical for the top plate due to the high compressive forces from
the bending moments at midВӯspan and the small vertical distance between the top plate
and the Sandwich Arch Bridge neutral axis. LM1 applied at the quarterВӯpoint was also
a designing load case regarding the required postВӯtensioning in the top plate due to the
negative bending moments.
To evaluate the competitiveness of the Sandwich Arch Bridge design regarding sustainВӯ
ability and construction process, the obtained optimal design was compared to a concrete
ThreeВӯSpan Bridge built at VosnГҰsvej in Denmark. The evaluation was done based on
the used materialвҖҷs global warming potential (GWP) and estimated construction time. The
GWP for both designs was similar for a span of 27m, with the Sandwich Arch Bridge deВӯ
sign having a saving of 7.75%. For increased span, 35m, the GWP for the Sandwich
Arch Bridge had a significantly lower GWP than the ThreeВӯspan bridge, a total of 21.63%
indicating that the Sandwich Arch Bridge design is favourable for increased spans above
27m. The GWP for pervious concrete used as fill material in the Sandwich Arch Bridge
design accounted for 46% of the total GWP for both spans. Using another more environВӯ
mentally friendly fill material, the Sandwich Arch Bridge design could be favourable for
reduced spans. Evaluating construction time for both designs, a time saving of вҲј45%
was estimated by using a Sandwich Arch Bridge design.
8.0.1
Future studies
Throughout the work of this thesis, several subjects were found which could be interesting
to investigate further to analyse the Sandwich Arch Bridge design and its possibilities.
вҖў Looking into the possibilities of optimising the shape of the top deck to reduce the
height of the filling towards the sides, further reducing weight and environmental
impact.
вҖў Further research and testing of the splitting force to evaluate the analytical suggesВӯ
tions presented in this thesis.

4.5 Max cable force:
Due to cable rise within the element, the internal stresses is found 
for the LAC section at the ends, with minimal cable rise and at 
the mid with max cable rise. These are controlled with the 
internal stresses in the concrete section and joint.

The LAC section at the ends, 200mm from the element end, is the dimentioning position. The cabel will have 
minimal rise at this point causing increased compression in the bottom of the section.

-> Max cable in PC1650 - SL270 = 13C15
The final stress in the concrete is dependent on may factors, and addition/removing of pre-stressing strands 
and increased length on concrete in the ends could increase the post-stressing capacity.  The maximum post-
tension cable dimension have been calculated in the same manner as above for all SL deck section heights and 
widths to present a optimal cable dimention to use in preliminary design.
The stress is calculated at half the section height which is conservative when looking at the effect of rise on 
the stress. No immediate post-tension loss have been included in the calculation of the stress.

1.8 Conclusion

1.7 N/M Diagram positive moment
NB! We see from the diagram that the capacity is lowest at the middle section related to bending 
moment. We therefore use the capacity of the middle section in future capacity checks of positive 
bending moment.
